Al-Sanusi's Theological Works
The editor lists al-Sanusi's main works, especially his creedal writings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key creedal works of Imam al-Sanusi and the context of their composition.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi's creedal works are the crown of his scholarship.

Before diving into al-Sanusi's theological arguments, it is helpful to know the key works he authored. His student al-Mallali compiled a list of his writings, focusing on his creedal treatises. This passage introduces three of his major works, including his first composition and his first work in the science of tawhid.

Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Let the list of works become clear.

The works are: al-Muqarrab al-Mustafi, Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid, and Umdat Ahl al-Tawfiq.
3

For each work, note what the passage says: the age when written, its significance, and its stylistic character.
The first creedal work is described as having 'solid and difficult' wording.
4

His works and his intellectual legacy: The writings of Imam al-Sanusi diversified in the Islamic library, and he is one of those who authored extensively. Despite this diversity, his creedal works were the crown of knowledge. His student Sheikh al-Mallali devoted a special chapter to them. You will see before you all the works he mentioned: (1) 'al-Muqarrab al-Mustafi fi Sharh Fara'id al-Hawfi': This is his first work. His age when he wrote it was nineteen or eighteen years, with some discrepancy. His story with the gnostic sheikh al-Hasan Abarkan will come, and his order to him to hide it until he reached forty years. (2) 'Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid' or 'al-‘Aqida al-Kubra': This is the first thing he composed in the science of tawhid. Its wording is solid and difficult, as stated in 'Sharh al-‘Aqida al-Wusta'. 'Umdat Ahl al-Tawfiq wa al-Tasdid' or 'Sharh al-‘Aqida al-Kubra': (1) See 'al-Mawahib al-Qudsiyya', folio 102. (2) See p. 45. (3) Thus Imam al-Sanusi himself used to name it. As for the addition in its original title: (The one extracted from the darkness of ignorance and the collar of blind following...) to the end, it is a description of it, not part of its name, as both al-‘Akkari and al-Hamidi see it, with al-Mallali's verification. (4) See 'Sharh al-‘Aqida al-Wusta', p. 123.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a classification and description of Imam al-Sanusi's literary output, focusing on his theological works. It begins by stating that al-Sanusi was a prolific author whose creedal ('aqida) writings are the crown of his scholarship. The editor notes that al-Sanusi's student Sheikh al-Mallali dedicated a special chapter to discussing these works. The passage then lists the works: The first is 'al-Muqarrab al-Mustafi fi Sharh Fara'id al-Hawfi', which was his earliest composition, written around age eighteen or nineteen, and there is a story connected with it involving Sheikh al-Hasan Abarkan, who instructed him to hide it until he turned forty. The second work is 'Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid', also called 'al-‘Aqida al-Kubra', which is al-Sanusi's first work in the science of tawhid. Its style is described as solid and difficult, as confirmed in his later commentary 'Sharh al-‘Aqida al-Wusta'. The third work listed is 'Umdat Ahl al-Tawfiq wa al-Tasdid' or 'Sharh al-‘Aqida al-Kubra'. The passage ends with editorial footnotes clarifying the title of the second work, noting that al-Sanusi himself called it simply 'Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid', and the longer descriptive phrase is not part of the official title. The passage thus provides bibliographic information and highlights the importance of al-Sanusi's creedal writings.

Imam al-Sanusi's Major Creedal Works
Enumeration of Sanusi's creedal works with descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchy and significance of Imam al-Sanusi's three main creedal works: al-Kubra, al-Wusta, and al-Sughra.

Core Idea
Sanusi's creedal works form a matched set of three — the Great, Middle, and Small — each with its own purpose.

Imam al-Sanusi authored many works, but his creedal writings are the crown of his scholarship. In this passage, his student al-Mallali enumerates them, beginning with his first composition and culminating in the famous 'Small Creed' — the most widely studied of all. Take note of how each work relates to the others in scope and difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the list of works. Notice the order from first to most famous.
The last work is introduced but the description is cut off.
2
Identify the three main creeds
The passage mentions al-Aqida al-Kubra, al-Wusta, and al-Sughra. Note their relative lengths: Kubra is longest and hardest, Wusta is medium, Sughra is shortest and most popular.
These are the core of Sanusi's creedal writings.
3
Observe the commentaries
Each major creed has a commentary by Sanusi himself. The commentary on the Sughra is also widely studied.
The commentary on the Sughra is known as 'Sharh Umm al-Barahin'.
4
Understand the fame of al-Sughra
The passage emphasizes that al-Sughra (Umm al-Barahin) is the most famous, with many commentaries and glosses. It is often referred to simply as 'the Sanusi creed'.
Al-Mallali's praise highlights its unparalleled status.
5
Reflect on the biographical detail
Notice that al-Sanusi wrote his first work at age 18-19 and was told to conceal it until 40. This shows his early scholarship and obedience to his teacher.

شرح العقيدة الوسطى » : وهو من عيون ما أُلف في علم التوحيد
- العقيدة الصغرى » المعروفة بـ « أم البراهين » و« ذات البراهين » و « السنوسية الصغرى » : وهي «عقائده » والمقصودة بقولهم : « عقيدة السنوسي » عند الإطلاق ، وكتب لها من الذيوع ما لم يكتب لغيرها ، وأقبل عليها العلماء شرحاً ، والطلاب حفظاً ودرساً ، قال العلامة الملالي : ( وهي من أجل العقائد ، ولا تعادلها عقيدة من عقائد من تقدم ولا من تأخر ، وقد أشار الشيخ رضي الله عنه إلى ذلك في صدر شرحه لها )
- شرح العقيدة الصغرى » ، ويعرف أيضاً بـ « توحيد أهل العرفان ومعرفة الله ورسله بالدليل والبرهان » ، وبـ « شرح أم البراهين » وهو أيضاً قد كتب له الذيوع والانتشار في الآفاق ، وكم من حاشية وضعت عليه
- صغرى الصغرى : وصفها الشيخ الملالي بقوله :
His authored works: The writings of Imam al-Sanusi diversified in the Islamic library, and he is one of those who wrote extensively. Despite this diversity, his creedal writings were the crown of knowledge. His student, Shaykh al-Mallali, devoted a special chapter to discussing them, and you will see before you all the works he mentioned; they are: (1) 'al-Muqrib al-Mustawfi fi Sharh Fara'id al-Hawfi': it is his first authored work, and his age when he wrote it was nineteen or eighteen years, with some uncertainty about that, and his story with Shaykh al-'Arif Billah al-Hasan Abarkan and his order to him to conceal it until he reaches the age of forty will come. (2) 'Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid' or 'al-Aqida al-Kubra': it is the first thing he composed in the science of tawhid, and its phrasing is firm and difficult as he stated in his commentary on 'al-Aqida al-Wusta'. 'Umdat Ahl al-Tawfiq wa al-Tasdid' or 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra': this commentary is considered one of the most extensive creedal books that the Imam authored. 'al-Aqida al-Wusta': it is more concise than 'al-Kubra' and above 'al-Sughra'. - 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta': it is among the best works authored in the science of tawhid. - 'al-Aqida al-Sughra' known as 'Umm al-Barahin', 'Dhat al-Barahin', and 'al-Sanusiyya al-Sughra': it is his 'creeds' and what is meant by their saying 'the Sanusi creed' when unqualified; it achieved a popularity that no other work achieved, and scholars turned to it for commentary, and students for memorization and study. Al-'Allama al-Mallali said: 'It is among the most noble creeds, and no creed of those before or after equals it, and the Shaykh, may Allah be pleased with him, alluded to that in the beginning of his commentary on it.' - 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Sughra', also known as 'Tawhid Ahl al-'Irfan wa Ma'rifat Allah wa Rusulihi bi al-Dalil wa al-Burhan', and as 'Sharh Umm al-Barahin'; it also achieved popularity and spread in the horizons, and how many glosses were written on it. - 'Sughra al-Sughra': al-Mallali described it by saying:
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and description of the major authored works of Imam Abu 'Abd Allah Muhammad b. Yusuf al-Sanusi (d. 895 AH/1490 CE), with a focus on his creedal writings. The text begins by noting the diversity of Sanusi's works, but emphasizes that his creedal works were the crown of his scholarship. It then mentions that his student Shaykh al-Mallali dedicated a chapter to listing these works. The enumeration begins with his first work, 'al-Muqrib al-Mustawfi fi Sharh Fara'id al-Hawfi', which is a commentary on a Hanafi inheritance text; the author's age at composition is given as eighteen or nineteen, with some uncertainty, and it recounts a story about being told to conceal it until age forty. Next is 'Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid' or 'al-Aqida al-Kubra' (the Great Creed), described as his first work in tawhid, with firm and difficult phrasing. Then 'Umdat Ahl al-Tawfiq wa al-Tasdid' or 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra', a commentary on the Great Creed that is among his most extensive creedal books. 'al-Aqida al-Wusta' (the Middle Creed) is more concise than the Great Creed but above the Small Creed. Its commentary, 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta', is praised as among the best works on tawhid. The most famous is 'al-Aqida al-Sughra' (the Small Creed), also called 'Umm al-Barahin' (Mother of Proofs), 'Dhat al-Barahin', and 'al-Sanusiyya al-Sughra'. It is the work commonly referred to as 'the Sanusi creed' when unqualified, and it achieved unprecedented popularity, with scholars commenting on it and students memorizing it. The passage quotes al-Mallali's high praise, saying no creed equals it. Its commentary, 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Sughra', also known as 'Tawhid Ahl al-'Irfan' or 'Sharh Umm al-Barahin', also spread widely and had many glosses. Finally, 'Sughra al-Sughra' (the Smallest of the Small) is mentioned but only introduced with al-Mallali's description, which is cut off here.

العقيدة الوسطى

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that al-Sughra is the only creedal work of Sanusi, ignoring al-Kubra and al-Wusta.
Correction: The passage lists al-Kubra and al-Wusta as major works with distinct characteristics; al-Sughra is the most famous but not the only one.

Checkpoint

B) العقيدة الوسطى

D) شرح العقيدة الصغرى

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Sanusi authored three main creedal works of decreasing size and increasing popularity, with al-Sughra (Umm al-Barahin) being the most widely studied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imam al-Sanusi wrote his first creedal work at eighteen or nineteen and was told to keep it hidden until forty. What does this tell you about the patience and readiness required to teach sacred knowledge? Are you quick to share what you learn before it has matured within you?
?
The Small Creed (al-Sughra) became far more popular than the larger, more detailed works. Reflect on what that suggests about the relationship between simplicity and impact. In your own practice, do you prefer deep study of detailed texts, or are you drawn to concise summaries? How does that choice affect your understanding of tawhid?

Life of Imam al-Sanusi
beginner

Sanusi's Major Creedal Works and Their Relationships
Lists Imam al-Sanusi's creedal works and their commentaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major creedal works of Imam al-Sanusi, their interrelationships, and the circumstances behind their composition.

Core Idea
Sanusi's creedal works form a graded system from concise to comprehensive.

Imam al-Sanusi's theological writings are foundational in Ash'ari kalam. This passage introduces his key creedal texts, explaining how they relate to each other and why each was written. By understanding this overview, you will appreciate the structure and purpose of his major works.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the works
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, highlighting each work mentioned. There are at least five distinct creed texts and their commentaries. Note the names: al-ʿAqīda al-Wusṭā, al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughrā (also called Umm al-Barāhīn), Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā, and al-Muqaddimāt.
Pay attention to the different titles for al-Ṣughrā.
2
Understand the hierarchical relationship
The works are arranged from longer to shorter: al-Wusṭā (intermediate) is between a larger (lost?) and the smaller; al-Ṣughrā is the widely known smaller creed; Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā is even more concise. Al-Muqaddimāt is a separate introductory text. Each creed has at least one commentary.
This graded approach helps students progress from easy to advanced.
3
Appreciate the personal story behind Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā
The passage tells that this shortest creed was written for Sanusi's elderly father, who found the Ṣughrā too heavy. It shows the practical aim of making theology accessible to all.
This illustrates Sanusi's pedagogical concern.

And this commentary is considered one of the most extensive creedal books authored by the Imam. 'The Intermediate Creed' (al-ʿAqīda al-Wusṭā): which is shorter than 'The Greater' and above 'The Smaller'. 'The Explanation of the Intermediate Creed' (Sharḥ al-ʿAqīda al-Wusṭā): it is one of the most excellent works composed in the science of tawḥīd. 'The Smaller Creed' (al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughrā), known as 'The Mother of Proofs' (Umm al-Barāhīn), 'Possessor of Proofs' (Dhāt al-Barāhīn), and 'The Sanusi Smaller': the pearl of his creeds, and intended by their saying 'The Sanusi Creed' when unqualified; and it attained a popularity unattained by others, and scholars engaged with it by commentary, and students by memorization and study. The scholar al-Mallali said: 'It is among the greatest creeds, and no creed of those who preceded or followed equals it, and the Shaykh, may Allah be pleased with him, referred to this in the beginning of his commentary on it.' 'The Explanation of the Smaller Creed' (Sharḥ al-ʿAqīda al-Ṣughrā), also known as 'The Tawḥīd of the People of Gnosis and Knowledge of Allah and His Messengers by Evidence and Proof', and as 'The Explanation of the Mother of Proofs'; it also attained wide circulation and spread in the regions, and how many glosses were written upon it. 'The Smaller of the Smaller' (Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā): al-Mallali described it saying: 'His abbreviated creed in extreme brevity, and it is smaller than the aforementioned Smaller Creed, and for this reason it is called the Smaller of the Smaller; and it was composed for his father, may Allah preserve him from every affliction and trial, and grant him the highest station. That was because when his father read the Smaller Creed to the Shaykh and completed it with commentary more than once, he found its study and memorization heavy due to his old age and many concerns, so he asked the Shaykh to make a creed smaller than the Smaller such that he could study and memorize it; so he made this creed and wrote it for him in his own hand. And the Shaykh pointed out in it exquisite subtleties and brilliant pearls that he did not mention in the previous creeds.' 'The Explanation of the Smaller of the Smaller' (Sharḥ Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā): it is a precious commentary that a student who seeks depth cannot do without; al-Mallali said: 'In it are wondrous benefits and strange subtleties.' 'The Introductions' (al-Muqaddimāt), also known as 'The Introduction' (al-Muqaddima); al-Mallali said: 'Among them is the Introduction that he composed as an explanation of his Smaller Creed, and it is close to it in size, and it is eight introductions that are an excellent aid for students.'
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a bibliographical overview of Imam al-Sanusi's major works in creed (ʿaqīda) and their commentaries. It begins by noting that the commentary being referenced (likely the current work) is among the most extensive. It then lists the 'Intermediate Creed' (al-ʿAqīda al-Wusṭā), positioned between the larger and smaller works. Next is its commentary (Sharḥ al-Wusṭā), praised as excellent. The core work is the 'Smaller Creed' (al-Ṣughrā), known by several names: 'Mother of Proofs' (Umm al-Barāhīn), 'Possessor of Proofs', and 'Sanusi Smaller'. This is the famous 'Sanusi Creed' that became immensely popular, studied by scholars and students worldwide. Al-Mallali's praise is quoted, emphasizing its uniqueness. Then comes its commentary (Sharḥ al-Ṣughrā), also widespread. Next is the 'Smaller of the Smaller' (Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā), an even more concise version written for the author's father who struggled with the longer text. Al-Sanusi included new insights not found in earlier creeds. Its commentary is described as indispensable. Finally, the 'Introductions' (al-Muqaddimāt) are listed, which are eight preliminary discussions explaining the Smaller Creed; al-Mallali notes they are close in size to the creed itself and very helpful for students. The passage is a valuable list of Sanusi's foundational texts.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may confuse Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā with al-Ṣughrā, thinking they are the same.
Correction: Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā is significantly shorter and was specifically written for the author's father due to his difficulty with al-Ṣughrā.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Sanusi authored multiple graded creedal texts, each with a commentary, to serve different levels of students.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Is there someone in your life—a parent, spouse, or friend—who struggles with a particular aspect of faith? ?
Sanusi's graded works show he cared deeply about making knowledge accessible. When you explain Islam to someone less knowledgeable, do you do so with patience and graded simplicity, or do you overwhelm them with details? What prevents you from returning to a basic text of ʿaqīda, studying it carefully, and adding your own reflections? Write down one verse or hadith that anchors your faith, and commit to memorizing it by next week.

Sanusi's Works as Recorded by al-Mallali
Bibliographical list of Sanusi's works with al-Mallali's notes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key works attributed to Imam al-Sanusi and how they are described in historical sources.

Core Idea
Sanusi's scholarly legacy is preserved through the eyewitness accounts of al-Mallali.

This passage offers a rare bibliographical glimpse into the works of Imam al-Sanusi, as recorded by the historian al-Mallali. It lists several of his major writings, including commentaries and a Qur'anic exegesis, and notes their state of completion. The opening couplet sets a spiritual tone, but the core is a curated inventory of Sanusi's scholarly output.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the structure: an opening couplet followed by a list of works.
The numbers in parentheses are footnote markers.
2
Identify each work and its description
Go through each entry in the list. For each work, note its title, subject, and what al-Mallali or the text says about it (e.g., completion status, difficulty).
There are five works mentioned after the couplet.
3
Reflect on the significance of this list
Ask yourself: What does this list tell us about Sanusi's scholarly interests? Why is it important that al-Mallali's eyewitness testimony is recorded?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a bibliographical listing of works attributed to Imam al-Sanusi, with descriptive notes and comments, primarily from the historian al-Mallali. It begins with an unexplained couplet followed by a commentary on two lines of Sufi poetry. Then it lists: 1) 'Sharh al-'Aqida al-Murshida' (a commentary on a creed), which al-Mallali saw complete in Sanusi's handwriting. 2) 'Al-Durr al-Manẓum fi Sharh Qawa'id Ibn Ajurrum', a grammatical commentary, also seen complete. 3) 'Sharh Jawahir al-'Ulum' in kalam, a commentary on a work by al-Iji following al-Bayḍawi's method, noted for its difficulty; al-Sanusi reportedly said of al-Bayḍawi's work that it is a sea compared to this book being a drop. 4) 'Tafsir al-Qur'an al-'Aziz', an incomplete Qur'an commentary reaching only to verse 5 of al-Baqara. 5) 'Tafsir Surat Sad' onwards, whose extent is unknown. The footnotes reference 'al-Mawahib al-Qudsiyya' for verification. The passage serves to document Sanusi's scholarly output.

?

Life of Imam al-Sanusi

Attribution of al-Ta'rifat: Sanusi's Definitions Compiled by al-Tha'alibi
Al-Mallali discusses the authorship of al-Ta'rifat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the book 'al-Ta'rifat' attributed to Imam al-Sanusi was actually compiled by his teacher, 'Abd al-Rahman al-Tha'alibi, from Sanusi's commentary on his own 'Aqida al-Sughra.

Core Idea
Not every work bearing Sanusi's definitions is by Sanusi himself.

Biographical notes on scholars often include discussions on the authenticity and authorship of works. In this passage, al-Mallali clarifies the status of 'al-Ta'rifat', a collection of definitions commonly associated with al-Sanusi. He explains that while the definitions are indeed Sanusi's, the compilation was made by his teacher al-Tha'alibi. This lesson will walk through that distinction and its significance for studying Sanusi's legacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full context of al-Mallali's remarks.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the key information about al-Ta'rifat is complete.
2
Identify the two categories of Sanusi's works
Al-Mallali first lists known works by Sanusi (answers, exhortations, copies), then transitions to works needing scrutiny. Find where this transition happens.
3
Analyze the attribution of 'al-Ta'rifat'
Focus on the sentence that states Sanusi is not the author but the source of the definitions. Note the role of al-Tha'alibi as compiler.
This is the central point of the lesson.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why distinguishing between author and compiler matters for studying Islamic texts and avoiding misconceptions.

قال الشيخ العلامة الملالي المؤرخ لسيرة الإمام السنوسي بعدما هذه التصانيف : ( فهذا ما علمنا من تأليفه رضي الله عنه : ما كتبه من الأجوبة على المسائل التي ترد عليه في جلّ الأوقات ، وبعض الأجوبة يحسن أن تعدها من تأليفه رضي الله عنه ؛ لكبرها واستقلالها بنفسها ، وما كتب من المواعظ والوصايا والرسائل والحُجُب التي يطلب فيها ، وما نسخ بيده من تصانيف العلماء ودواوين القدماء ((٢)
-
وثَمَّ كتب فيها تأمل عند نسبتها إليه ، فليقع الكلام عليها : ويعرف أيضاً بـ « الحقائق في حقائق السنوسي » ، التعريفات (٣) : ما تراه من الحدود والتعريفات في هذه الرسالة جلُّه للإمام السنوسي ، ولكنه ليس هو المؤلف لهذه الرسالة ، وإنما هي مادة لشيخه الإمام الحافظ عبد الرحمن الثعالبي الهواري ، وقد جمع رسالته هذه من شرح العقيدة الصغرى لتلميذه الإمام السنوسي ، هذا الشرح الذي اعتنى فيه الإمام ببيان كل مصطلح دائر في علم الكلام ، ويظهر أنَّ جمعها قد راقَ لشيخه الإمام الثعالبي ، ولكن لما
Al-Shaykh al-Allama al-Mallali, the historian of the life of Imam al-Sanusi, said after these works: (This is what we know of his authorship, may Allah be pleased with him: what he wrote of answers to questions that came to him most of the time, and some answers it is good to count as his authorship, may Allah be pleased with him, due to their size and independence; and what he wrote of exhortations, wills, epistles, and veils [or proofs] that are sought in them; and what he copied by his hand of the works of scholars and the diwans of the ancients (2). -
Then there are books regarding which there is reflection when attributing them to him, so let the discussion fall on them: It is also known as 'al-Haqa'iq fi Haqa'iq al-Sanusi', al-Ta'rifat (3): what you see of definitions and definitions in this treatise, most of it belongs to Imam al-Sanusi, but he is not the author of this treatise; rather it is material for his shaykh, Imam al-Hafiz 'Abd al-Rahman al-Tha'alibi al-Hawari, and he collected this treatise of his from the commentary on al-'Aqida al-Sughra by his student Imam al-Sanusi, this commentary in which the Imam took care to explain every term current in the science of kalam, and it appears that its collection pleased his shaykh Imam al-Tha'alibi, but when
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a concluding note by the biographer al-Mallali about Sanusi's works, followed by a discussion of a specific book, 'al-Ta'rifat'. First, al-Mallali summarizes what he knows of Sanusi's authored output: answers to questions, some of which are large enough to be considered independent works; exhortations, wills, epistles, and 'hujub' (perhaps 'proofs' or 'veils'); and copies he made of other scholars' works. Then he transitions to books whose attribution requires scrutiny. One such book is 'al-Ta'rifat', also known as 'al-Haqa'iq fi Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'. He explains that although most of the definitions in this treatise are from Sanusi, Sanusi is not its author. Rather, the material was collected by his teacher, 'Abd al-Rahman al-Tha'alibi al-Hawari, from Sanusi's commentary on 'al-'Aqida al-Sughra', a commentary in which Sanusi explained kalam terminology. Al-Tha'alibi compiled these definitions into a separate work, which pleased him. The passage ends mid-sentence with 'but when', indicating the original text continues beyond the page.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The book 'al-Ta'rifat' was authored by Imam al-Sanusi himself.
Correction: The passage states that Sanusi is not the author; rather, the definitions are his but were compiled by his teacher al-Tha'alibi from Sanusi's commentary.

D) Al-'Allama al-'Aḍud al-Iji

Key Takeaway
The definitions in 'al-Ta'rifat' are Sanusi's work, but the compilation was made by his teacher al-Tha'alibi, so the book is not directly authored by Sanusi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that al-Tha'alibi saw value in his student's definitions and compiled them for others. Is there someone whose knowledge or insight you have neglected to preserve or share? What one specific action can you take this week to support, pass on, or encourage a beneficial piece of Islamic knowledge from someone else?
?
Al-Sanusi's definitions live on through his teacher's compilation, even though he did not author the final book. Are you actively ensuring that your own good deeds and knowledge benefit others beyond your lifetime? If not, what is one concrete step you can take—such as writing, teaching, or documenting—to make your knowledge accessible to future generations?
?
If your teacher or student collected your work and attributed it to you, how would you feel? Are you more concerned with personal recognition or with the benefit that knowledge brings to the Ummah? Honestly examine your intentions and ask Allah to purify them.

The Beauty and Scholarly Reception of Sanusi's Creed
Praise of Sanusi's creed and scholarly quotes on its qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi's creed is highly regarded by scholars, focusing on its stylistic qualities and the testimonies of its authority.

Core Idea
Sanusi's creed is praised for its beauty, precision, and being preserved from error.

This lesson presents the commendation of Imam al-Sanusi's creed itself, highlighting its eloquence and conciseness. It then shares the endorsements of two major scholars who attest to its flawless expression and universal acceptance. This establishes the text's credibility before delving into its theological content.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening praise
Read the first line: 'حسن عبارة هذه العقيدة ووجازتها وقرب مأخذها'. Notice the three qualities highlighted: beauty of expression, conciseness, and accessibility of its source.
These qualities set the tone for why this text is valued.
2
Note the editorial clarification
The passage includes editorial notes about Sanusi's other works and a warning against false attributions. Recognize that these are not part of Sanusi's original text but helpful context.
This prevents confusion about the scope of Sanusi's writings.
3
Examine the scholarly quotes
Read the quotes from al-Shifshāwīnī and al-Habṭī. Al-Shifshāwīnī emphasizes that no critic can find fault, and al-Habṭī states Sanusi's speech is 'preserved from slips'. Reflect on the weight of these endorsements.
These quotes establish the text's authority and reliability.

Commentary
This passage combines praise for the book itself with editorial notes about Sanusi's other works. It opens by lauding the beauty, conciseness, and accessibility of the creed (العقيدة) being studied. The text then inserts editorial remarks listing some of Sanusi's writings: a work on logic (شرح الموجهات) and a medical commentary on Ibn Sina's Urjuza. It warns that some other titles are falsely attributed. The main praise comes from two scholars: al-Shifshāwīnī emphasizes the brilliance and precision of Sanusi's expression, noting that critics cannot find fault and that great scholars accept his works. Al-Habṭī adds that Sanusi's speech is 'preserved from slips' (محفوظ من السقطات), meaning free from error. This establishes the authority of Sanusi's text.

Sanusi's Noble Character
Description of Sanusi's virtuous character traits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the noble character traits of Imam al-Sanusi as described in this biographical passage, and how his character reflects his spiritual authority.

Core Idea
Imam al-Sanusi's luminous character combined knowledge, mercy, and humility.

This lesson introduces the personal character of Imam al-Sanusi. The passage presents a beautiful portrait of a scholar whose inner virtues complemented his vast knowledge. Before we study his theological arguments, it's important to appreciate the spiritual character that earned him respect and authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the translation. Notice the poetic tone and the list of traits.
The passage begins with a metaphor about pitching tents in faith.
2
Identify the key traits
Go through each character trait mentioned. See if you can find the Arabic terms for each.
Traits are given in pairs or clusters.
3
Reflect on the summary
The passage concludes with a statement about his luminous essence. Ask yourself why the author ends with this summary.
This connects his inner state to divine care.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قبس من عظيم اخلاق
جمع الله تعالى للإمام السنوسي إلى عظيم علمه وبحبوحة معرفتِه .. سعةَ الأخلاق الحميدة ، فجابَ شُعب الإيمان وطنَّبَ فيها
خيامه ، فعُهدَ عنه أنه خيرٌ برٌّ ، تقي ورع زاهد ، حلیم صبور ، سكنتِ الرحمة فؤاده ، وغلبت عليه الشفقة على جميع خلق الله تعالى ، هين
لين القياد ، شديد الحياء ، شديد الحياء ، كثير التواضع ، قليل الكلام والضحك ، تجلّى عليه الخوف من الله تعالى ؛ فأكثر من إحياء الليل مناجياً ، وأكثر من الصدقة والدعاء راجياً .
وبالجملة : كانت طينته نورانية ، وسيرته محفوفة بعناية ربانية .
A spark from his great character. God Almighty gathered for Imam al-Sanusi, along with his great knowledge and ample understanding, a broad range of praiseworthy character traits. He traversed the branches of faith and pitched his tents therein. He was known to be good, pious, God-fearing, ascetic, forbearing, patient. Mercy settled in his heart, and compassion for all of God's creation dominated him. He was gentle, easy to lead, extremely shy, extremely shy, very humble, of little speech and laughter. Fear of God Almighty manifested upon him, so he frequently revived the night with intimate prayer, and increased in charity and supplication, hoping. In summary: his essence was luminous, and his biography was encompassed by divine care.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a description of Imam al-Sanusi's character. It begins by stating that God combined immense knowledge with noble ethics. A poetic phrase follows: 'He traversed the branches of faith and pitched his tents therein,' indicating his deep immersion in religious virtues. Then a list of specific traits: he was good (خير) and pious (بر), God-fearing (تقي), ascetic (ورع), forbearing (حلیم), patient (صبور). Mercy filled his heart, and he had overwhelming compassion for all creation. He was gentle (هين), easy-going (لين القياد), extremely shy (شديد الحياء), very humble (كثير التواضع), spoke and laughed little. The fear of God led him to frequent night prayer and charity. The summary states his essence was luminous (نورانية) and his life surrounded by divine care. This establishes his spiritual authority.

Testimonies to Sanusi's Vast Knowledge
Praise of Sanusi's knowledge by two scholars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two prominent scholars testified to the vastness of Imam Sanusi's knowledge, emphasizing his role as a mujaddid and his mastery of both exoteric and esoteric sciences.

Core Idea
Sanusi's vast knowledge was recognized by scholars as a renewal of religion.

This lesson presents two scholarly testimonies that establish Imam Sanusi's towering stature in both exoteric and esoteric Islamic sciences. Al-Shifshāwīnī and al-Ḥadīkī are quoted directly from their works, providing a window into how Sanusi was perceived by his contemporaries. The passage not only praises his output but also highlights his unique position in tawḥīd and taṣawwuf.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand the meaning.
Notice the two distinct testimonies.
2
Identify the two scholars and their key descriptions
Find what al-Shifshāwīnī and al-Ḥadīkī each said about Sanusi. Compare their emphases.
3
Connect the testimonies to Sanusi's legacy
Reflect on why these testimonials are significant for establishing Sanusi as a major figure in Islamic theology and Sufism.
This relates to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما سعة علمه :
فآثاره العلمية الواسعة تشهد له بذلك ، ولا سيما علم التوحيد
والتصوف
قال فيه العلامة الشفشاوني : ( كان من مشايخ المئة التاسعة ، وتوفي على رأسها ، فكان من جدَّد لهذه الأمة أمر دينها على رأس تلك المئة كما أخبر الصادق المصدوق صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وكان من أكابر الأولياء ، وأعلام العلماء ، وتآليفه تدلُّ على تحقيقه وغزارة علمه ((۱)
وقال العلامة الحضيكي : ( كان آية في العلم والصلاح والهدى والزهد والورع ، له أوفر حظ في العلوم فروعها وأصولها ، إذا تحدث في علم ظنّ السامع أنه لا يحسن غيره ، لا سيما في التوحيد ، وانفرد بعلم الباطن ، لا يقرأ شيئاً من علم الظاهر إلا خرج لعلوم الآخرة ، (۲)
As for the vastness of his knowledge: his extensive scholarly works testify to that, especially the sciences of tawḥīd (monotheism) and taṣawwuf (Sufism). The scholar al-Shifshāwīnī said concerning him: 'He was among the shaykhs of the ninth century, and died at its beginning, so he was among those who renewed for this nation the affairs of its religion at the beginning of that century, as the truthful and believed (Prophet) صلى الله عليه وسلم informed, and he was among the greatest saints and prominent scholars, and his works indicate his verification and abundance of knowledge.' And the scholar al-Ḥadīkī said: 'He was a sign in knowledge, righteousness, guidance, asceticism, and piety; he had the greatest share in the sciences, both branches and roots; when he spoke on a science, the listener would think he knew nothing else, especially in tawḥīd; and he was unique in the esoteric science; he would not read anything of the exoteric science without it leading to the sciences of the Hereafter.'
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage testifies to Sanusi's vast knowledge. It opens by stating that his extensive scholarly works bear witness, particularly in tawḥīd and taṣawwuf. Two major scholars are quoted. Al-Shifshāwīnī describes him as a renewer (mujaddid) of the religion at the beginning of the ninth century, a great saint, and a prominent scholar; his works demonstrate his verification (تحقيق) and abundance of knowledge. Al-Ḥadīkī adds that Sanusi was a 'sign' (آية) in knowledge, righteousness, and asceticism; he had mastery over all sciences; when he spoke, listeners thought he knew nothing else—especially in tawḥīd; he was uniquely distinguished in esoteric knowledge (علم الباطن) and every exoteric study led him to the sciences of the Hereafter. This establishes his authority in both exoteric and esoteric domains.

Key Terms
علم التوحيد
علم التصوف
المئة التاسعة
مجدد
آية في العلم
علم الباطن
علم الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Shifshāwīnī said Sanusi died at the end of the ninth century.
Correction: The phrase 'توفي على رأسها' means he died at the beginning of the century.

Checkpoint

A) He was a renewer of religion.
B) He was a sign in knowledge and esoteric science.
C) He wrote many books on logic.
D) He taught only tawḥīd.

Key Takeaway
Two scholars, al-Shifshāwīnī and al-Ḥadīkī, testified that Imam Sanusi was a renewer of religion and a sign of knowledge, mastering both exoteric and esoteric sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi's knowledge led others to think he knew nothing else when he spoke on a subject. When you speak about Islam, does your speech reflect deep mastery or superficiality? What would it take for you to become a 'sign' (āyah) in knowledge?
?
Al-Ḥadīkī noted that Sanusi's study of exoteric sciences always led him to the Hereafter. Are your studies or readings primarily for this world or do they lead your heart to the next? Examine your recent learning—what was its ultimate aim?
?
Both scholars highlight not only Sanusi's knowledge but his righteousness, asceticism, and piety. Are you seeking knowledge without cultivating character? If a gap exists between your learning and your actions, what is one concrete step you can take today to align them?

The Author's Character, Knowledge, and Asceticism
Biographical sketch of the author's virtues, knowledge, and asceticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author's character, knowledge, and asceticism reflect his spiritual station and why his biography is included in a theological primer.

Core Idea
A scholar's life is the first proof of his knowledge.

Before diving into the theological arguments, the author's own biography serves as a living example of the virtues he champions. This passage paints a picture of his character, his mastery of the sciences, and his profound asceticism, supported by the words of notable scholars. As you read, consider how these qualities relate to the study of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without pausing; then read the translation. Let the author's own words and the testimonies settle in your mind.
The first word is uncertain; it likely means 'His characteristics'.
2
Identify the three main sections
The passage divides into character, knowledge, and asceticism. Notice the transitions: 'As for the breadth of his knowledge...' and 'As for his asceticism...'.
3
Study the scholarly testimonies
Al-Shafshawani, al-Hadiki, and Ibn Maryam each praise the author. Focus on what each testimony adds: revival, mastery of both outward and inner sciences, and the author's unique ability to derive spiritual lessons from outward study.
4
Reflect on the author's self-definition of a scholar
The author says a true scholar 'finds difficulty in what is clear and clarifies what is difficult.' Ask yourself how this relates to the depth of understanding needed in kalam.
5
Consider the significance of his asceticism
The author's statement about the gnostic saint is striking: he would not even turn to Paradise if unveiled. Think about how this detachment underscores the goal of tawhid – focus on Allah alone.

خيامه[؟] ، فعُهدَ عنه أنه خيرٌ برٌّ ، تقي ورع زاهد ، حلیم صبور ، سكنتِ الرحمة فؤاده ، وغلبت عليه الشفقة على جميع خلق الله تعالى ، هين لين القياد ، شديد الحياء ، كثير التواضع ، قليل الكلام والضحك ، تجلّى عليه الخوف من الله تعالى ؛ فأكثر من إحياء الليل مناجياً ، وأكثر من الصدقة والدعاء راجياً . وبالجملة : كانت طينته نورانية ، وسيرته محفوفة بعناية ربانية . أما سعة علمه : فآثاره العلمية الواسعة تشهد له بذلك ، ولا سيما علم التوحيد والتصوف . قال فيه العلامة الشفشاوني : ( كان من مشايخ المئة التاسعة ، وتوفي على رأسها ، فكان من جدَّد لهذه الأمة أمر دينها على رأس تلك المئة كما أخبر الصادق المصدوق صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وكان من أكابر الأولياء ، وأعلام العلماء ، وتآليفه تدلُّ على تحقيقه وغزارة علمه ) . وقال العلامة الحضيكي : ( كان آية في العلم والصلاح والهدى والزهد والورع ، له أوفر حظ في العلوم فروعها وأصولها ، إذا تحدث في علم ظنّ السامع أنه لا يحسن غيره ، لا سيما في التوحيد ، وانفرد بعلم الباطن ، لا يقرأ شيئاً من علم الظاهر إلا خرج لعلوم الآخرة ، لا سيما في التفسير والحديث ، كأنه يشاهد الآخرة ؛ لكثرة مراقبته لله تعالى ) . وقد جعل رحمه الله تعالى للعالم علامات يعرف بها ؛ حيث قال : ( العالم حقاً : من يستشكل الواضح ، ويوضح المشكل ؛ لسعة فهمه وعلمه وتحقيقه ، فهو الذي يُحضر مجلسه ، ويُستمع فوائده ) . وقال العلامة ابن مريم في البستان : ( أما علومه الظاهرة فله فيها أوفر نصيب ، وجمع من فروعهـا وأصـولهـا السهـم والتعصيب ) . وأما زهده وورعه : فحسبك بكلمته التي تنوقلت في ترجمته ؛ حيث قال : ( حقيقة الولي العارف : من لو كشف له عن الجنة وحورها ما التفت إليها ، ولا ركن لغيره تعالى ) ، فمن زهد بمثل هذا فهو عما دونه أزهد .
His characteristics: so it was known about him that he was good, pious, God-fearing, devout, ascetic, forbearing, patient; mercy dwelt in his heart and compassion for all of God's creatures prevailed over him; gentle, easy of disposition, intensely shy, very humble, little in speech and laughter; fear of God manifested upon him; so he increased in reviving [part of] the night in intimate prayer, and increased in charity and supplication hoping. In summary: his clay was luminous, and his conduct was encompassed by divine care. As for the breadth of his knowledge: his extensive scholarly works testify to that for him, especially the science of tawhid and tasawwuf. Al-Allama al-Shafshawani said about him: 'He was among the shaykhs of the ninth century, and died at its head; he was one who revived for this community the matter of its religion at the head of that century, as the Truthful and Verified [Prophet], peace be upon him, informed; and he was among the greatest of the awliya and the prominent scholars, and his writings indicate his verification and abundance of knowledge.' And al-Allama al-Hadiki said: 'He was a sign in knowledge, righteousness, guidance, asceticism, and piety; he had the greatest share in the sciences, their branches and roots; when he spoke about a science, the listener thought he knew nothing else, especially in tawhid; and he was unique in the science of the inner dimension; he would not read anything from the outward sciences except that it would bring forth the sciences of the afterlife, especially in tafsir and hadith, as if he witnessed the afterlife due to the abundance of his watchfulness of God, the Exalted.' And he—may God have mercy on him—set signs for the scholar by which he is known, where he said: 'The true scholar is he who finds difficulty in what is clear and clarifies what is difficult, due to the breadth of his understanding, knowledge, and verification. So he is the one whose gathering is attended and whose benefits are listened to.' And al-Allama Ibn Maryam said in al-Bustan: 'As for his outward sciences, he had the greatest share in them, and he combined their branches and roots, the lot and the portion.' And as for his asceticism and piety: it suffices you with his statement that was transmitted in his biography, where he said: 'The reality of the gnostic saint is: if the Garden and its houris were unveiled to him, he would not turn to them, nor would he rely on anyone other than God, the Exalted.' So whoever is ascetic about such a thing is even more ascetic about what is beneath it.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a biographical sketch of the author, describing his character, knowledge, and asceticism. It begins by listing his virtues: goodness, piety, fear of God, asceticism, forbearance, patience, compassion, gentleness, humility, and fear of God, which led him to increase in night worship and charity. The summary states that his nature was luminous and his conduct guarded by divine care. The next section focuses on the breadth of his knowledge, citing testimonies from two scholars. Al-Shafshawani attests that he was a reviver of the religion in the ninth century, a great saint and scholar, and that his writings prove his depth and abundance of knowledge. Al-Hadiki adds that he was a sign in knowledge and piety, with a unique mastery of tawhid and the inner sciences; his study of outward sciences always yielded spiritual fruits. The author himself then defines a true scholar as one who can clarify difficult issues and is widely attended. Ibn Maryam testifies to his comprehensive mastery of the outward sciences. The final section on asceticism quotes the author's own words: a true saint (wali) would be so absorbed in God that even the promise of Paradise would not distract him. The passage concludes that if he was ascetic about Paradise, he was even more ascetic about lesser things.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Biographical details are irrelevant to theology.
Correction: In traditional Islamic learning, the author's piety and knowledge establish his authority; his life exemplifies the qualities needed to engage with tawhid.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The author's life of virtue, mastery of both outward and inner sciences, and profound asceticism qualify him as a reliable teacher of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author's definition of a true scholar is 'one who finds difficulty in what is clear and clarifies what is difficult.' When you study, do you assume you already understand the obvious? What is one 'clear' aspect of your faith that you have not really grappled with? How will you approach it differently today?
?
The author said a gnostic saint would not turn to Paradise and its houris if unveiled. Examine your heart: Do you worship Allah primarily for His reward or for His own sake? What would change if you could say, like the author, that you would not turn to Paradise?
?
The passage describes the author as having intense shyness and humility. In your daily interactions, where do pride and self-assertion creep in? Commit to one specific situation this week where you will practice humility, even if it costs you something.

Sanusi's Definition of the True Scholar
Sanusi defines the true scholar and his characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of a true scholar as one who questions the clear and clarifies the unclear.

Core Idea
The true scholar is defined by depth, not data.

Sanusi gave a distinctive definition of the true scholar, emphasizing depth of understanding over mere accumulation of knowledge. This lesson unpacks his words and shows how he combined breadth of learning with intellectual humility.

Guided Path
1
Read Sanusi's definition
Read the Arabic passage where Sanusi defines the true scholar. Note the qualities he mentions: questioning the clear and clarifying the unclear.
2
Reflect on the meaning
Consider what it means to 'question the clear.' This implies a deep probing mind that does not take things at face value, and the ability to make difficult concepts accessible.
3
Connect to his own example
Ibn Maryam's quote shows that Sanusi himself mastered both branches and roots of the sciences, embodying his definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد جعل رحمه الله تعالى للعالم علامات يعرف بها؛ حيث قال: (العالم حقاً: من يستشكل الواضح، ويوضح المشكل؛ لسعة فهمه وعلمه وتحقيقه، فهو الذي يُحضر مجلسه، ويُستمع فوائده) وقال العلامة ابن مريم في البستان: (أما علومه الظاهرة فله فيها أوفر نصيب، وجمع من فروعهـا وأصـولهـا السهم والتعصيب). وأما زهده وورعه: فحسبك بكلمته التي تنوقلت في ترجمته؛ حيث قال: (حقيقة الولي العارف: من لو كشف له عن الجنة وحورها ما التفت إليها، ولا ركن لغيره تعالى)، فمن زهد بمثل هذا فهو عما دونه أزهد.
And he (may Allah have mercy on him) placed for the scholar signs by which he is known, as he said: 'The true scholar is the one who questions the clear and clarifies the unclear; due to the breadth of his understanding, knowledge, and verification. He is the one whose session is attended and whose benefits are listened to.' And the scholar Ibn Maryam said in al-Bustan: 'As for his outward sciences, he has the greatest share in them, and he has combined their branches and roots with abundant skill.' As for his asceticism and piety, suffice it to know his statement that was transmitted in his biography, where he said: 'The reality of the knower-wali is that if he were shown Paradise and its houris, he would not turn to them nor rely on other than Allah Almighty.' So whoever is ascetic in such a manner is even more ascetic in what is less than it.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a biographical description that presents two definitions from Sanusi: first, the definition of a true scholar, and second, the definition of a true wali (saint). The passage opens by stating that Sanusi set signs for recognizing the scholar, quoting him directly. The scholar is one who questions that which is clear and clarifies that which is unclear, due to his deep understanding, extensive knowledge, and precise verification. Such a person is worthy of being attended and listened to. The biographer then adds a supporting quote from Ibn Maryam praising Sanusi's mastery of the outward sciences. Next, the passage turns to Sanusi's asceticism and piety, quoting his famous statement about the true 'arif-wali: if shown Paradise and its houris, he would not look at them nor rely on anything but Allah. The passage concludes that one who is so detached from Paradise itself is even more detached from lesser worldly things.

Checkpoint

A) One who memorizes many texts

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defines the true scholar not by the quantity of his knowledge but by his depth, as shown by his ability to problematize the obvious and clarify the ambiguous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a clear, well-known Islamic ruling, do you accept it without deeper thought, or do you pause to question its underlying wisdom as a true scholar would? What would it take for you to cultivate that depth?
?
Your gatherings with others—are they places where real benefit is shared and sought, or are they merely social routines? Imagine sitting in a session of a true scholar: would your heart be as eager to listen as Sanusi describes?
?
Sanusi says the true scholar has 'breadth of understanding, knowledge, and verification.' Where in your own learning are you merely collecting information versus truly verifying and understanding? What is one step you will take today to move from accumulation to depth?

Sanusi's Description of the True Wali
Sanusi describes the true wali as indifferent to Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's description of the true wali as one who is indifferent even to Paradise, focusing only on Allah.

Core Idea
The true wali is detached from everything, even Paradise itself.

Sanusi's asceticism was legendary. In this passage, he gives a striking definition of the true 'arif-wali: one who would not turn to Paradise and its houris if shown them, let alone worldly pleasures. This lesson explores that deep detachment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on asceticism
Read the Arabic passage describing Sanusi's zuhd and wara‘ (asceticism and piety). Notice his famous statement about the true wali.
2
Analyze the example
Consider why Sanusi mentions Paradise and houris specifically. He is showing that the wali's focus is solely on Allah, not even on the highest reward.
3
Compare to lesser detachment
The passage concludes: if he is so detached from Paradise, he is even more detached from lesser things. This underscores his extreme level of spiritual refinement.

Sanusi's Avoidance of Worldly Authority
A series of anecdotes illustrating Sanusi's rejection of worldly power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extent of Sanusi's detachment from worldly power through several anecdotes.

Core Idea
Sanusi's piety required distance from rulers and officials.

This lesson shares several stories from Sanusi's life that illustrate his extreme caution in dealing with those in authority. The anecdotes show his consistent refusal to associate with the powerful, even when it meant inconvenience or altering his plans. As you read, notice the variety of situations and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdotes carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the literal translation. Allow the distinct episodes to stand out.
2
Identify the common theme
Notice that in every story, Sanusi actively avoids contact with worldly people, especially those with power. The details differ but the pattern is consistent.
3
Reflect on the motive
Ask yourself why Sanusi went to such lengths. The commentary and common misunderstanding will help clarify that it was not misanthropy but piety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بعث إليه السلطان في أخذ شيء من غلات مدرسة الولي الصالح الحسن أبركان، فامتنع، فألحوا عليه، فكتب في الاعتذار كتابة مطولة، فقبل منه. كان لا يأنس بأحدٍ ولا يتسبب في معرفته، وقد تبغض إليه الاجتماع بأهل الدنيا والنظر إليهم وقربهم، وهو لمن بسط لهم فيها ونالوا رتبها.. أشدُّ بغضاً، قال تلميذه الملالي: (خرج يوماً معنا للصحراء، فرأى فرساناً بثياب فاخرة على بعد، فقال: من هؤلاء؟ قلنا: خواص السلطان، فتعوذ ورجع لطريق آخر). ولقيهم مرة أخرى، وما تمكن من الرجوع، فجعل وجهه للحائط وغطاه حتى جازوا ولم يروه. ولما أراد ختم التفسير عزم على قراءة سورة (الإخلاص) يوماً و(المعوذتين) يوماً، فسمع به الوزير وأراد حضور الختم، فبلغه ذلك، فقرأ السور الثلاثة يوماً واحداً؛ خيفة حضور الوزير عنده ورؤيته والاجتماع به. وطلبه السلطان أن يطلع إليه ويقرأ التفسير بحضرته على عادة المفسرين، فامتنع، فألحوا عليه، فكتب إليه معتذراً بغلبة الحياء له، ولا يقدر على التكلم هناك، فأيسوا منه.
The sultan had sent to him (requesting) to take something from the crops of the school of the righteous wali al-Hasan Abrak, so he refused. They insisted, so he wrote a lengthy apology, and it was accepted from him. He was not comfortable with anyone nor did he seek to be acquainted, and he detested the company of people of the world, looking at them, and being near them. And he had an even greater hatred for those who were given prosperity in it and attained high ranks. His student al-Mallali said: 'One day he went out with us to the desert, and he saw horsemen in splendid clothes in the distance, so he asked: Who are these? We said: Close associates of the sultan. So he sought refuge and turned back to another path.' And he met them another time, and he was not able to turn back, so he put his face to the wall and covered it until they passed and did not see him. When he wanted to complete the tafsir, he resolved to recite Surah al-Ikhlāṣ one day and al-Muʿawwidhatayn another day. The minister heard and wanted to attend the completion, so upon learning that, he recited the three surahs in one day, fearing the minister's presence at his place, seeing him, and meeting him. The sultan asked him to come up to him and recite the tafsir in his presence, as was the custom of the exegetes, so he refused. They insisted, so he wrote to him apologizing that shyness overcomes him and that he cannot speak there, so they gave up on him.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents a series of anecdotes illustrating Sanusi's extreme avoidance of worldly people, especially those in power. It begins with an episode in which the sultan asked him to take some crops from a school, but Sanusi refused and wrote a long apology. Then a general statement is made about his character: he was not at ease with anyone and detested the company of worldly people, especially the prosperous. His student al-Mallali narrates that one day they saw the sultan's horsemen in the distance; Sanusi sought refuge and turned away. Another time, unable to turn back, he covered his face against a wall to avoid being seen. When he planned to complete his tafsir over two days, the minister wanted to attend, so Sanusi recited all three surahs in one day to avoid his presence. Finally, when the sultan repeatedly asked him to recite the tafsir in person, Sanusi refused, apologizing that shyness prevented him from speaking. These anecdotes collectively demonstrate his deep reluctance to engage with worldly authority.

Key Terms
زهد
سلطان
وزير
دنيا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Sanusi's avoidance was due to misanthropy or dislike of people.
Correction: His avoidance was driven by piety and a desire to protect his religious practice from the distractions and temptations of worldly power.

Checkpoint
Why did Sanusi recite the three surahs in one day instead of over two days?
A) He was in a hurry
B) He feared the minister would attend the completion ceremony
C) He forgot his plan
D) He wanted to surprise his students

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's life exemplified the principle that true piety requires one to be wary of the distractions of worldly power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you sought the approval of people in positions of power or influence, even at the cost of your spiritual peace? What would it take for you to turn away from that path as Sanusi did?
?
Sanusi covered his face to avoid being seen by the sultan's men. What comfort or status are you afraid to lose that keeps you from distancing yourself from worldly people? Are you willing to feel that loss for the sake of your deen?
?
If a person of authority asked you to do something that would compromise your devotion, would you have the courage to refuse and write a lengthy apology, or would you comply to avoid tension? What is holding you back from living with that level of piety?

Anecdotes of Asceticism: Avoiding Worldly Association
Biographical anecdotes on asceticism and avoidance of worldly people

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the scholar's extreme asceticism manifested in his avoidance of worldly people and association, and the principles behind his actions.

Core Idea
Asceticism means turning away from worldly allure, not rejecting all gifts.

This lesson presents vivid biographical anecdotes that illustrate the scholar's commitment to asceticism (zuhd). Through these stories, you will see how he avoided the powerful, refused their gifts, and even altered his routines to prevent contact with them. Yet, his asceticism was nuanced: he accepted gifts from those with lawful earnings. These anecdotes bring the abstract concept of zuhd to life.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdotes
Read through the Arabic and translation, noting the variety of situations described.
Pay attention to the scholar's reactions to different worldly figures.
2
Identify core patterns
Look for recurring themes: avoidance of rulers, refusal of gifts, and fear of association.
The scholar consistently avoids the powerful and their favors.
3
Understand the nuance
Notice that the scholar did accept gifts from non-rulers with lawful earnings. This shows his asceticism was not absolute rejection but discerning.
This nuance corrects a common misunderstanding about zuhd.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why the scholar acted this way: to maintain his devotion and fear of God, and to protect his worship from worldly distractions.
The passage ends with a transition to his worship and devotion.

الصالح الحسن أبركان ، فامتنع ، فألحوا عليه ، فكتب في الاعتذار كتابة مطولة ، فقبل منه (۱) كان لا يأنس بأحدٍ ولا يتسبب في معرفته ، وقد تبغض إليه الاجتماع بأهل الدنيا والنظر إليهم وقربهم ، وهو لمن بسط لهم فيها ونالوا رتبها .. أشدُّ بغضاً ، قال تلميذه الملالي : ( خرج يوماً معنا للصحراء ، فرأى فرساناً بثياب فاخرة على بعد ، فقال : من هؤلاء ؟ قلنا : خواص السلطان ، فتعوذ ورجع لطريق آخر ) . ولقيهم مرة أخرى ، وما تمكن من الرجوع ، فجعل وجهه للحائط وغطاه حتى جازوا ولم يروه (٢) . ولما أراد ختم التفسير عزم على قراءة سورة ( الإخلاص ) يوماً و ( المعوذتين ) يوماً ، فسمع به الوزير وأراد حضور الختم ، فبلغه ذلك ، فقرأ السور الثلاثة يوماً واحداً ؛ خيفة حضور الوزير عنده ورؤيته والاجتماع به (٣) . وطلبه السلطان أن يطلع إليه ويقرأ التفسير بحضرته على عادة المفسرين ، فامتنع ، فألحوا عليه ، فكتب إليه معتذراً بغلبة الحياء له ، ولا يقدر على التكلم هناك ، فأيسوا منه . وإذا سمع يوماً بوليمة أحد أبناء الدنيا تخلّف يومه عن الحضور ؛ خيفة أن يُدعى ، فلا يظهرُ بالكلية حتى تمر أيام الوليمة وربما تخلف قبله أياماً . وحاشى نفسه عن قبول عطايا السلطان ومن لاذ به ، وربما تأتي داره وهو غائب ، فإذا وجدها أنكر على أهل داره ، وتغيَّر كثيراً ، وكان يقبل هدايا غيرهم ممَّن طابت أكسابهم ، ويدعو لهم . وقد جاءه يوماً ابن الخليفة ومعه عبدٌ ، فأقبل يقبل يديه ورجليه ، وطلب منه قبوله هدية ، فأبى ، ثم تبسم مطيِّباً قلبه ودعا له ، فطلب منه أن يأخذه ويتصدق به على الفقراء ، فأبى ولم يرضَ لهم ذلك . ولعله كان يُقصد بشفاعات وقضاء حوائج ، ويُطلب منه الكتابة إلى الأمراء ، فكان يكره ذلك ، وإن فعل فعن حياء ؛ فقد كلَّفه إنسان يوماً بكَتْبِ ثلاثين براءة ، فكتبها وقال : هذه مصائب ابتلينا بها . وكان يتمنى ألا يرى أحداً وألا يراه أحدٌ ، وكان يقول : لا حاجة لي بأحد ولا بماله (١) . وقد قال فيه العلامة الحوضي : ( من الكامل ) كم جاءَتِ الدنيا تسوقُ رئاسة يبغي إليك تقـربـاً أبناؤها فأبيت عنها معرضاً مستحقراً لم يخدعَنْكَ جمالها وبهاؤها . وأما عبادته وتبتله وخوفه من الله تعالى ، وحسن معاملته مع عباده :
The righteous, the good, al-Hasan Abarkan, so he refused, so they insisted upon him, so he wrote a lengthy apology, and it was accepted from him (1). He would not feel intimate with anyone nor cause himself to be known to them, and he detested association with people of the world, looking at them, and being near them, and he was even more averse to those for whom [worldly goods] were spread out and who attained ranks therein. His student al-Mallali said: 'He went out with us one day to the desert, and saw horsemen in luxurious clothes in the distance, and he said: 'Who are these?' We said: 'The sultan's elite.' So he sought refuge [in God] and returned by another path.' He encountered them another time and was unable to turn back, so he turned his face to the wall and covered it until they passed and did not see him (2). When he wanted to complete the commentary [on the Qur'an], he resolved to recite Surat al-Ikhlas one day and al-Mu'awwidhatayn another day. The vizier heard of it and wanted to attend the completion, so when that reached him, he recited the three surahs in a single day; fearing the vizier's presence with him, seeing him, and meeting with him (3). The sultan asked him to come to him and recite the commentary in his presence, as was the custom of commentators, so he refused, and they insisted upon him, so he wrote to him excusing himself due to overwhelming shyness, and that he could not speak there, so they despaired of him. And if he heard one day of a banquet of one of the worldly people, he would stay away that day from attending; fearing that he would be invited, so he would not appear at all until the days of the banquet passed, and sometimes he would stay away days before it. He kept himself above accepting the gifts of the sultan and those who sought refuge with him, and sometimes [the gift] would come to his house while he was absent, and if he found it, he would reproach his household and become very upset. He would accept gifts from others whose earnings were good, and he would pray for them. One day the caliph's son came to him with a servant, and he came forward kissing his hands and feet, and asked him to accept a gift. He refused, then smiled, soothing his heart, and prayed for him. He asked him to take it and give it in charity to the poor, but he refused and was not pleased with that. He was often sought for intercessions and fulfillment of needs, and was asked to write to the amirs, but he disliked that. If he did it, it was out of shyness. One day a man tasked him with writing thirty letters of acquittal, so he wrote them and said: 'These are calamities we have been afflicted with.' He wished that he would not see anyone and that no one would see him. He used to say: 'I have no need of anyone nor of his wealth' (1). The scholar al-Hawdi said in [the meter] al-Kamil: 'How many times did the world come driving leadership, its sons seeking nearness to you, but you turned away from it, disdainful and contemptuous; its beauty and splendor did not deceive you.' As for his worship, his devotion, his fear of God Almighty, and his good treatment of His servants:
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a series of biographical anecdotes illustrating the extreme asceticism and avoidance of worldly entanglements of the scholar (likely a reference to the author Sanusi or a figure he admires). The passage opens mid-story with a refusal and apology, then proceeds through multiple specific instances. First, we learn that the scholar did not seek intimacy with anyone and detested the company of worldly people, especially those with high rank and luxury. His student al-Mallali recounts that when they saw richly dressed horsemen, the scholar sought God's refuge and took another path. On another occasion, he turned his face to the wall and covered himself so as not to see them. The anecdotes highlight his fear of encountering powerful people: when the vizier wanted to attend the completion of his Qur'an commentary, he recited all three surahs in one day to avoid the vizier's presence. He also refused the sultan's request to recite before him, excusing himself due to overwhelming shyness. He would avoid banquets of worldly people to the point of hiding for days. He refused gifts from the sultan and his associates, reproaching his household if such gifts arrived, but he accepted gifts from others with lawful earnings and prayed for them. When the caliph's son offered a gift, he refused even the option of giving it to the poor. He disliked being sought for intercessions or writing to amirs, and when forced, he called it a calamity. He wished to be unseen and unseeing, declaring his independence from people and their wealth. The scholar al-Hawdi praised him in poetry, describing how he turned away from worldly leadership despite its allure. The passage ends with a promise to cover his worship and devotion, but cuts off incomplete.

Key Terms
زهد (asceticism)
تبتل (devotion)
حياة (shyness)
أهل الدنيا (people of the world)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: True asceticism requires rejecting all gifts from everyone.
Correction: The scholar rejected gifts from rulers and their associates but accepted gifts from others whose earnings were lawful, showing that asceticism is about avoiding worldly entanglements, not absolute rejection.

Checkpoint
Why did the scholar recite Surat al-Ikhlas and al-Mu'awwidhatayn in a single day instead of over three days?
A) He forgot his original plan.
B) He wanted to finish the commentary quickly.
C) He feared the vizier's presence and meeting with him.
D) He was in a hurry to travel.

Key Takeaway
The scholar's asceticism was characterized by a profound avoidance of worldly people and their favors, but he accepted gifts from those with lawful earnings, demonstrating a nuanced understanding of zuhd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see people of wealth, status, or influence, does your heart incline towards them, seeking their approval or company, or do you feel a similar aversion as the scholar? What does your reaction reveal about your attachment to this world? Will you take a concrete step today to distance yourself from a worldly association that weakens your devotion?
?
The scholar avoided even the possibility of being invited to a worldly banquet by staying away for days. Are there social gatherings or events you attend that subtly erode your focus on the Hereafter? What would it cost you—and what would it gain you—to abstain from one such gathering this month?
?
The scholar refused gifts from the sultan but accepted from those with lawful earnings. Do you carefully examine the source of the gifts, favors, or income you receive? Are you willing to decline something beneficial if it comes from a questionable source, trusting that God will provide from pure means? What is one such benefit you need to reconsider?

Life of Imam al-Sanusi
beginner

Biography of a Pious Scholar: Asceticism and Fear of God
Biographical portrait of a saintly scholar's life and character.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the scholar's asceticism, fear of God, and compassion were manifest in his life and conduct.

Core Idea
True piety combines fear of God with compassion for His creation.

This lesson presents a biographical portrait of a saintly scholar whose life exemplified the balance between ascetic withdrawal and active compassion. The passage illustrates his extreme caution towards worldly power, his profound fear of God, and his gentle treatment of all people. Through specific anecdotes and descriptions, we see how he combined inner devotion with outer kindness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the narrative flow before interpreting.
The passage is a continuous biographical description, not a theological argument.
2
Identify examples of asceticism
Find instances where the scholar avoids worldly power, refuses gifts, or seeks solitude. Note his attitude towards the sultan and intercessions.
Asceticism here includes both material renunciation and avoidance of influence.
3
Observe the fear of God
Look for descriptions of his fear, such as moaning during dhikr, prolonged sorrow, and his constant remembrance of the afterlife.
His fear is not paralyzing but motivates devotion.
4
Note his compassion and character
Highlight his kindness to children, humility, acceptance of lawful gifts, and his preaching suited to each listener.
Compassion balances ascetic withdrawal.
5
Reflect on the balanced piety
Consider how the scholar combined all these qualities and why he was considered the imam of his time.
The goal is to understand the holistic nature of his piety.

الحضور ؛ خيفة أن يُدعى ، فلا يظهرُ بالكلية حتى تمر أيام الوليمة وربما تخلف قبله أياماً وحاشى نفسه عن قبول عطايا السلطان ومن لاذ به ، وربما تأتي داره وهو غائب ، فإذا وجدها أنكر على أهل داره ، وتغيَّر كثيراً ، وكان يقبل هدايا غيرهم ممَّن طابت أكسابهم ، ويدعو لهم وقد جاءه يوماً ابن الخليفة ومعه عبدٌ ، فأقبل يقبل يديه ورجليه ، وطلب منه قبوله هدية ، فأبى ، ثم تبسم مطيِّباً قلبه ودعا له ، فطلب منه أن يأخذه ويتصدق به على الفقراء ، فأبى ولم يرضَ لهم ذلك ولعله كان يُقصد بشفاعات وقضاء حوائج ، ويُطلب منه الكتابة إلى الأمراء ، فكان يكره ذلك ، وإن فعل فعن حياء ؛ فقد كلَّفه إنسان يوماً بكَتْبِ ثلاثين براءة ، فكتبها وقال : هذه مصائب ابتلينا بها . وكان يتمنى ألا يرى أحداً وألا يراه أحدٌ ، وكان يقول : لا حاجة لي بأحد ولا بماله (١) وقد قال فيه العلامة الحوضي : ( من الكامل ) كم جاءَتِ الدنيا تسوقُ رئاسة يبغي إليك تقـربـاً أبناؤها فأبيت عنها معرضاً مستحقراً لم يخدعَنْكَ جمالها وبهاؤها وأما عبادته وتبتله وخوفه من الله تعالى ، وحسن معاملته مع عباده : فكان في هذا إمام زمانه ، وقد أثرت فيه صحبة الصوفية وعلماء الحقائق ، فطاب خبره و مخبره ، وحَسُنت سيرته وسريرته كان رضي الله عنه طويل الحزن ، كثير الخوف ، ولشدة خوفه يسمع لصدره أنين و وهو مستغرق في الذكر ، فلا يشعر بمن معه ، مع تواضع وحسن خلق ورقة قلب ، رحيماً متبسماً في وجه من لقيه ، مع إقبال وحسن كلام ، يتزاحم الأطفال على تقبيل أطرافه ، ليناً هيناً حتى في مشيه ، ما ترى أحسن خلقاً ، ولا أوسع صدراً ، ولا أكرم نفساً ولا أعطف قلباً ، ولا أحفظ عهداً منه ؛ يوفّر الكبير ، ويقف مع الصغير ، ويتواضع للضعفاء ، مُعظَّماً جانب النبوة في غاية ، حتى ارتحل الناس إليه وتبرَّكوا به . لا يعارضه أحد إلا أفحمه ، جمع له العلم والعمل والولاية إلى النهاية ، مع شفقته على الخلق وقضاء حوائجهم عند السلطان ، له من القبول والهيبة والإجلال في القلوب ما لم ينله غيره من علماء عصره وزُهّاده وكان جلُّ وعظه للناس في الخوف من المولى الجليل ومراقبته ، وذكر أحوال الآخرة ، وهو إلى هذا يعظ كل أحدٍ بما يناسب حاله ، وقل أن تراه إلا وهو يحرّك شفتيه بذكر الله تعالى (١) ومن صور شفقته وخوفه من مولاه سبحانه : أنه مرَّ به ذئب يطارده
He attended [gatherings] for fear of being called upon, so he would not appear at all until the days of the feast had passed, and perhaps he would be absent days before. He kept himself away from accepting gifts from the sultan and those who sought refuge with him. Perhaps his house would come while he was away, and if he found it [with something], he would rebuke his household and change greatly. He would accept gifts from others whose earnings were lawful, and would pray for them. One day the son of the caliph came to him with a servant, and began kissing his hands and feet, and asked him to accept a gift, but he refused. Then he smiled, comforting his heart, and prayed for him. He asked him to take it and give it in charity to the poor, but he refused and was not pleased with that for them. Perhaps he was sought for intercessions and fulfilling needs, and was asked to write to the princes, but he disliked that, and if he did it, it was out of shyness. One day a man asked him to write thirty letters of safe-conduct, so he wrote them and said: 'These are calamities with which we have been tested.' He wished that he would not see anyone and that no one would see him. He used to say: 'I have no need of anyone nor his wealth.' The scholar al-Hawdi said about him: (from the perfect meter) 'How often the world came driving leadership, its sons seeking close approach to you, but you refused it, turning away, disdainful; its beauty and splendor did not deceive you.' As for his worship, devotion, and fear of God Almighty, and his good treatment of His servants: in this he was the imam of his time. The company of Sufis and scholars of realities had an effect on him, so his inner and outer character became good, and his conduct and inner state improved. He, may God be pleased with him, was of prolonged sorrow, abundant fear. Due to the intensity of his fear, a moaning could be heard from his chest while he was engrossed in dhikr, so he was unaware of those with him, along with humility, good character, tenderness of heart. He was merciful, smiling in the face of those he met, with welcome and good speech. Children would crowd to kiss his limbs. He was gentle and easy-going even in his walk. You would not see anyone of better character, nor broader of chest, nor nobler of soul, nor more affectionate of heart, nor more faithful to a covenant than him. He honored the old, stood with the young, and humbled himself before the weak, magnifying the prophetic station to the utmost, until people traveled to him and sought blessings from him. No one opposed him but he silenced him. He combined knowledge, action, and sainthood to the utmost, with compassion for creation and fulfilling their needs with the sultan. He had such acceptance, awe, and reverence in hearts as none other of the scholars and ascetics of his time attained. Most of his preaching to people was about fear of the Majestic Lord and mindfulness of Him, and mentioning the states of the Hereafter. Yet he would preach to each person according to what suited his state. Hardly would you see him except moving his lips in remembrance of God Almighty. Among the images of his compassion and fear of his Lord, Exalted is He: a wolf passed by him chasing him...
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a biographical portrait of a saintly scholar, highlighting his extreme asceticism, fear of God, compassion for people, and spiritual stature. The narrative begins by describing his avoidance of worldly leaders and their gifts: he would hide during feasts to avoid being called upon, refused gifts from the sultan, and even rebuked his household if they accepted anything in his absence. He accepted gifts only from those with lawful earnings. A specific anecdote with the caliph's son illustrates his refusal and eventual gentle refusal of a gift for charity. He disliked intercessions and writing letters for princes, but sometimes complied out of shyness, once writing thirty letters and calling them 'calamities.' He preferred solitude. A poetic eulogy by al-Hawdi praises his rejection of worldly leadership. The passage then turns to his worship and devotion: he was an imam in his time, influenced by Sufis and scholars of realities. His fear of God caused him to moan during dhikr, and he was known for prolonged sorrow and fear. His character was gentle: humble, smiling, kind to all, including children. He honored the old, humbled himself before the weak, and magnified prophethood. People traveled to him for blessings. He combined knowledge, action, and sainthood, and had great acceptance and awe in hearts. His preaching focused on fear of God and the afterlife, tailored to each listener. He was constantly in dhikr. The passage concludes with a specific instance of his compassion: a wolf chased him, likely teaching a lesson of mercy, though the sentence is incomplete.

Correction: This is a biographical illustration of piety, not a doctrinal argument. It serves as an example, not a proof.

Key Takeaway
This scholar combined rigorous asceticism and fear of God with profound compassion and humility, setting an example of balanced piety.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This scholar would hide during feasts to avoid being called upon for gifts. What feast or occasion in your life are you attending or seeking recognition from, even though your heart knows it compromises your sincerity? Will you take a step to avoid it this week?
?
His fear of God was so intense that a moaning could be heard from his chest during dhikr. When was the last time your worship made you physically tremble or weep? If not, what is blocking that depth of awe in your private prayers?
?
Children would crowd to kiss his limbs because of his gentle character. Who in your life—your child, spouse, neighbor, or coworker—feels safe enough to approach you? If no one, what pride or harshness must you remove to become approachable?
