Imam al-Sanusi's Defense of Karamat
Al-Sanusi affirms karamat, nearly equates them with miracles, and criticizes some Sunni jurists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Sanusi's doctrinal position on karamat, including his comparison to prophetic miracles and his critique of deniers.

Core Idea
Karamat are real and nearly reach the level of prophetic miracles, but some Sunni jurists wrongly deny them.

Imam al-Sanusi aligns with mainstream Sunni verificationists in affirming the reality of karamat. He even says they almost reach the level of prophetic miracles. But he criticizes some Sunni jurists who go too far in denying them, considering belief in such phenomena as disbelief. This lesson presents his golden words from his commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between 'muhaqqiqun' (verifiers) and 'some jurists'.
2
Identify his main claim about karamat
Find where al-Sanusi compares karamat to prophetic miracles. What does he say about their status?
He uses the phrase 'takadu talhaqu' (almost reach).
3
Examine his critique of deniers
He criticizes two groups: people of innovation and some Sunni jurists. What is his specific criticism of the jurists?
He expresses wonder ('al-ajab') at their extreme position.
4
Reflect on his humble closing
Read his final words about being 'on the shore of longing'. What does this teach about the proper attitude toward saints?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب الإمام السنوسي في الكرامة ووقوعها مذهب عامة المحققين من أهل السنة والجماعة ، وقد نثر الحديث عنها مختصراً تارة ومطولاً أخرى في ( سنوسياته ( المباركة ، وله عبارة ذهبية نقدية لم تحجم قدرة الله تعالى ولم تقصر من شأن الكرامة في « شرح العقيدة الوسطى » إذ قال فيها : من ( فكرامات الأولياء باعتبار ظهورها تكاد تلحق بمعجزات الأنبياء وإنكارها ليس بعجيب من أهل البدع والأهواء ؛ إذ لم يشاهدوا ذلك أنفسهم قط ) إلى أن قال : ( وإنما العجب من بعض فقهاء أهل السنة ؛ حيث قال فيما روي عن إبراهيم بن أدهم أنهم رأوه بالبصرة يوم التروية وفي ذلك اليوم بمكة : إنَّ من اعتقد جواز ذلك يكفر !! )(٢) . ولك أن تتأمل كلمته المتواضعة المنكسرة حينما حكى شروط الولي التي ذكرها الإمام ابن دهاق ؛ إذ قال : ( ونحن بالنسبة إلى هذا المقام مقامِ أولياء الله تعالى وخاصة حضرته .. على ساحل التمني ،
And the school (madhhab) of Imam al-Sanusi concerning karamat and their occurrence is the school of the general body of verifiers (muhaqqiqun) from the Ahl al-Sunna wa'l-Jama'a. He has scattered the discussion about it briefly sometimes and at length other times in his blessed 'Sanusiyyat'. He has a golden and critical expression which does not diminish the power of Allah Almighty nor belittle the status of karamat in his 'Sharh al-'Aqida al-Wusta', where he said: 'The karamat of the saints, by virtue of their appearance, almost reach the level of the miracles of the prophets. Denying them is not surprising from the people of innovation and desires, since they have never witnessed them themselves.' Then he said: 'But the wonder is from some jurists of the Ahl al-Sunna, who said concerning what was narrated from Ibrahim ibn Adham that they saw him in Basra on the day of Tarwiya and on that same day in Mecca: "Whoever believes in the permissibility of that becomes a disbeliever!!"' (2) And you may contemplate his humble and broken words when he narrated the conditions of the saint (wali) which Imam Ibn Dahhaq mentioned, when he said: 'We, in relation to this station, the station of the saints of Allah and the special ones of His Presence ... are on the shore of longing.'
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Sanusi's doctrinal position on karamat (the miraculous events granted to saints). He states that his madhhab aligns with the mainstream Sunni verificationist tradition. He then quotes his own 'golden words' from his work 'Sharh al-'Aqida al-Wusta', in which he makes two key points: first, the karamat of the saints, in terms of their evidentiary power, are almost comparable to the miracles of the prophets, and denial of them is expected from innovators who have never witnessed such things. Second, he expresses astonishment at certain Sunni jurists who considered belief in the possibility of a saint being in two places at once (like Ibrahim ibn Adham) as disbelief. Imam al-Sanusi criticizes this extreme view, implying that such denial itself is problematic. He ends with a humble remark about our lowly station relative to the saints, quoting his own words of longing. The passage thus defends the reality of karamat and warns against excessive rejection.

Key Terms
كرامة
معجزة
أهل السنة والجماعة
محققون
فقهاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Sunni scholars accept karamat without exception.
Correction: This passage shows that some Sunni jurists denied karamat to the point of declaring disbelief, and Imam al-Sanusi critiques their denial.

Checkpoint
How does Imam al-Sanusi describe the karamat of the saints in relation to the miracles of the prophets?
A) They are identical in every way.
B) They are completely different and cannot be compared.
C) By virtue of their appearance, they almost reach the level of prophetic miracles.
D) They are inferior and have no evidentiary value.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Sanusi affirms the reality of karamat, elevates them nearly to the level of prophetic miracles, and criticizes those who go to extremes in denying them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a story of a saint's miracle because it seemed too extraordinary? How does al-Sanusi's warning against denying karamat challenge your own instinct to reject what seems unlikely? Take a moment to repent if you have been quick to deny without evidence.
?
Al-Sanusi says he is 'on the shore of longing' regarding the station of the saints. Do you truly long for closeness to Allah through His friends, or are you content with a distant faith? What concrete step can you take today to move from the shore toward the deep?
?
If you were to hear a respected scholar declare that believing in a certain karamat makes you a disbeliever, would you follow blindly? How does this lesson equip you to evaluate such claims and protect your heart from extremes?

Sanusi's Vision of Hell and Its Effect
Sanusi relates his vision of Hell and the lasting effect on him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the profound impact of a spiritual vision of Hell on Imam al-Sanusi and the lesson it carries for believers.

Core Idea
A vision of Hell transformed Sanusi's state, showing the reality of the Hereafter.

This lesson presents a biographical account of Imam al-Sanusi's personal experience. He was seen pale and distressed, and upon inquiry, he revealed that Allah had shown him a vision of Hell. This story teaches us the seriousness of the afterlife and how it affects the hearts of the righteous.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the story.
Notice the emotional language: 'contracted, changed in color'.
2
Identify the cause and effect
Identify what caused Sanusi's change in state and the lasting effect mentioned.
The cause is the vision of Hell; the effect is ongoing sadness and change.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Consider what this story teaches about the reality of the Hereafter and the response of the righteous.
It shows that even great scholars are deeply affected by such realities.
4
Address misunderstanding
Some may doubt such visions; understand that they are possible and can be a divine mercy.
This is based on the passage itself – he did not claim it was a dream but a vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك : أنه رئي يوماً كثير الانقباض متغير اللون ، فسئل عن ذلك ، فأجاب بعد إلحاح وشرط كتم الأمر ، فقال : أطلعني الله تعالى على رؤية جهنم وما فيها ، نعوذ بالله منها ، فمن حينئذ صرت أتغير وأحزن إلى الآن ، فهذا سبب تغيري (1)
From that: He was seen one day very contracted (with sadness), changed in color, so he was asked about that, and he answered after insistence and after stipulating concealment of the matter, saying: 'Allah, Exalted, informed me of seeing Hell and what is in it — we seek refuge in Allah from it — and from that time I began to change and grieve until now. This is my reason for change.'
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a biographical anecdote recounting a personal spiritual experience of Imam al-Sanusi. He was observed to be very sad and pale, and after being pressed, he revealed that Allah showed him a vision of Hell and its contents. Since that time, he has been in a state of sadness. This illustrates the profound effect of contemplating the afterlife and the reality of divine punishment on the hearts of the righteous. It also shows the Imam's humility and his concealment of such experiences except when necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Final Days of Imam al-Sanusi
A student’s account of his teacher’s final days, his withdrawal, and his last attempt to reach the mosque.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Illness forced al-Sanusi to withdraw, but his love for the community compelled him to return.

This lesson presents a touching account from al-Sanusi's student about his teacher's last days. Despite intense physical suffering and a natural inclination to withdraw, al-Sanusi forced himself to go to the mosque when he learned the people missed him. His lament at the mosque door reveals his humility and reliance on Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the narrative flow.
Notice the emotional tone: the student is describing a deeply personal memory.
2
Identify al-Sanusi's change in behavior
The passage says he became 'greatly contracted towards people' and found it hard to go out. Understand that this was due to his illness, not a spiritual change.
The phrase 'كثير الانقباض عن الخلق' is key.
3
Understand his motivation for returning
Al-Sanusi forced himself to go to the mosque when he heard the people were saddened by his absence. His action shows his care for the community.
He did this despite his physical difficulty.
4
Reflect on his final moment at the mosque
At the mosque door, he could not ascend and lamented to Allah. This shows his humility and acceptance of his weakness.
His words 'How can I ascend to the mosque, O Lord?' are a poignant expression of reliance on Allah.

اللوحة الأخيرة من حياته لندع الشيخ محمد بن عمر الملالي تلميذ الإمام السنوسي يرسم لنا هذه اللوحة وهو يحدثنا عن ساعات اللقاء والرحيل عن هذه الفانية إلى الدار الباقية ، قال رحمه الله تعالى : كان رضي الله عنه في أواخر عمره كثير الانقباض عن الخلق ، لا يكاد ينبسط مع أحد كما كانت عادته قبل ذلك ، ويشق عليه الخروج إلى المسجد للإقراء والصلاة ، ولا يخرج إليه في بعض الأيام إلا حياءً من الناس الذين ينتظرونه في المسجد للصلاة عنه في أواخر عمره كثير الانقباض عن الخلق ، ولمَّا أحس رضي الله تعالى عنه بألم مرضه الذي توفي منه . . انقطع عن المسجد ، فسمع الناس بمرضه ، فصاروا يأتون إلى المسجد فلا يجدونه ، فتتغير قلوبهم من فقدان الشيخ وعدم رؤيته لهم ، فأُخبر الشيخ بذلك ، فصار يتكلف الخروج إلى المسجد للصلاة لأجل الناس ، فإذا رأوه فرحوا وسُرُّوا بخروجه ورؤيته . فخرج يوماً وأتى لباب المسجد ، وأراد الصعود إليه ، فلم يقدر ، فقال : كيف أطلع إلى المسجد يا ربِّ ؛ أو كما قال ، فهم بالرجوع
The final tableau of his life. Let Shaykh Muhammad ibn Umar al-Mallali, the student of Imam al-Sanusi, draw for us this tableau, as he narrates to us about the hours of meeting and departure from this transitory world to the eternal abode. He (al-Mallali) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'He (al-Sanusi), may Allah be pleased with him, was in his last days greatly contracted towards people, hardly would he open up with anyone as was his custom before that. It became difficult for him to go out to the mosque for teaching and prayer, and he would not go out on some days except out of shyness of the people who were waiting for him in the mosque for prayer. He was in his last years greatly contracted towards people. When he felt the pain of his illness from which he died, he cut off from the mosque. The people heard of his illness, so they would come to the mosque and not find him; their hearts would change from the loss of the shaykh and not seeing him. So the shaykh was informed of that, and he began to force himself to go out to the mosque for prayer for the sake of the people. When they saw him, they rejoiced and were delighted by his going out and seeing him. So one day he went out and came to the door of the mosque, intending to ascend to it, but he could not. He said: "How can I ascend to the mosque, O Lord?" or words to that effect, and he contemplated returning.'
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a biographical narrative by al-Sanusi's student al-Mallali describing his teacher's final days. It portrays al-Sanusi as becoming increasingly withdrawn and uncomfortable in public due to his illness. Despite his reluctance, he forced himself to go to the mosque because the people missed him. However, his physical weakness overcame him, and at the mosque door he could not ascend the steps, lamenting to his Lord. This shows the Imam's deep care for his community and his humility even in hardship.

انقباض
المسجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Sanusi's withdrawal was due to spiritual negligence or disinterest in the community.
Correction: His withdrawal was a result of physical illness and his absorption in divine matters; he was forced to retreat by his body, not his spirit.

?
The people grieved deeply over al-Sanusi's absence. Are there people in your life—family, friends, community—who depend on your presence in the masjid? Are you truly aware of the impact your absence might have on their spiritual state?

Imam Sanusi's Final Days: Lessons in Steadfastness
Al-Mallali narrates Imam Sanusi's final illness and death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the final days of Imam Sanusi and derive spiritual lessons from his illness, patience, and unwavering faith.

Core Idea
True piety is revealed in how one faces death: with steadfastness and trust in Allah.

The final days of a great scholar often distill the essence of their teachings. Imam Sanusi's last days, as narrated by his student al-Mallali, show a man whose faith remained unshaken even as his body failed. This story invites us to reflect on our own relationship with Allah and the world, especially at life's end.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full story. Pay attention to the sequence of events and Imam Sanusi's reactions.
The narrative is rich in detail, so reading it twice may help.
2
Reflect on his withdrawal and concern for others
Notice how he initially withdraws but forces himself to pray with the community out of concern for their sadness. This shows his selflessness.
This illustrates the concept of ihsan (excellence) even in hardship.
3
Consider his vision and the command to 'leave'
His vision of the lowest heaven and the command to leave his current state indicates divine preparation for death.
The 'prison' he mentions might refer to a worldly attachment he gave up.
4
Learn from his patience in suffering
Despite severe pain and weakness, he remained conscious and without complaint. This is a model of sabr (patience) and rida (contentment with Allah's decree).
This connects to the Islamic teaching of accepting death with dignity.

اللوحة الأخيرة من حياته
لندع الشيخ محمد بن عمر الملالي تلميذ الإمام السنوسي يرسم لنا هذه اللوحة وهو يحدثنا عن ساعات اللقاء والرحيل عن هذه الفانية إلى الدار الباقية ، قال رحمه الله تعالى :
الله ( كان رضي لا يكاد ينبسط مع أحد كما كانت عادته قبل ذلك ، ويشق عليه الخروج إلى المسجد للإقراء والصلاة ، ولا يخرج إليه في بعض الأيام إلا حياءً من الناس الذين ينتظرونه في المسجد للصلاة عنه في أواخر عمره كثير الانقباض عن الخلق ،
ولمَّا أحس رضي الله تعالى عنه بألم مرضه الذي توفي منه . . انقطع عن المسجد ، فسمع الناس بمرضه ، فصاروا يأتون إلى المسجد فلا يجدونه ، فتتغير قلوبهم من فقدان الشيخ وعدم رؤيته لهم ، فأُخبر الشيخ بذلك ، فصار يتكلف الخروج إلى المسجد للصلاة لأجل الناس ، فإذا رأوه فرحوا وسُرُّوا بخروجه ورؤيته
•
فخرج يوماً وأتى لباب المسجد ، وأراد الصعود إليه ، فلم يقدر ، فقال : كيف أطلع إلى المسجد يا ربِّ ؛ أو كما قال ، فهم بالرجوع إلى داره فبدا له خوفاً من أن يدخل على الناس حزناً برجوعه ، فتكلف الصعود إلى المسجد ، وصلى بالناس صلاة عصر يوم الجمعة ، ولم يكمل الصلاة إلا بشق النفس ، وهذه آخر صلاة صلاها
•
فرجع إلى داره ، فبقي إلى صبيحة يوم السبت من الغد ، فقربت إليه زوجته طعاماً ، فقال لها : لا أقدر على شيء شيء(١) ، فقالت له : وأيُّ شيء بك ؟ فقال لها : أنا تخلفت ، ثم غاب عن حسّه ، فبقي على تلك الحالة النهار كله .
ثم كلمته زوجته وقالت له : ما الذي غيَّبك عن حِسّكَ ؟ أو قريباً من هذا ، فقال لها : إنَّ الملائكة قد صعدت بي إلى السماء الدنيا ، فسمعت قائلاً يقول لي : اترك ما أنت عليه فقد قَرَّبَ أجلُكَ ، ثم قال : لا أستطيع أن أفسر لك بقية ما رأيت ، أو كما قال
فقالت له زوجته : وما الذي أُمرت بتركه ؟ قال لها : قد تركت حبس ذلك المحبس لا آخذ منه شيئاً أبداً
.
ثم إنه لازم الفراش من حينئذ إلى أن توفي ، ومدة مرضه عشرة أيام ، وفي كل ساعة يتقوَّى مرضه وتضاعف ألمه وتضعف قوته وحركته ، ويثقل لسانه ، وهو مع ذلك ثابت العقل [لم] يتأوه ولا أنَّ
،
(١) وقد ذكر الملالي في ( المواهب القدسية » ( ق ١٣ ) أنه بلغ به الجهد حتى عجز عن التيمم وأركان الصلاة ، فقال : ( رحم الله تعالى أبا حنيفة حيث قال بسقوطها ) يعني : الصلاة .
The last panel of his life
Let us call upon the Shaykh Muhammad ibn Umar al-Mallali, the student of Imam al-Sanusi, to draw for us this panel as he tells us about the hours of meeting and departure from this transitory world to the everlasting abode. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said:
Allah ( he was, may Allah be pleased with him, hardly ever cheerful with anyone as was his custom before that, and it was difficult for him to go out to the mosque for teaching and prayer, and he would not go out to it on some days except out of shyness of the people who were waiting for him in the mosque for prayer. In his later life he was very withdrawn from people.
When he (may Allah be pleased with him) felt the pain of the illness from which he died, he stopped going to the mosque. The people heard of his illness and would come to the mosque but not find him, and their hearts changed from the loss of the Shaykh and not seeing him. So the Shaykh was informed of that, and he began to force himself to go out to the mosque for prayer for the sake of the people. When they saw him they rejoiced and were delighted by his coming out and seeing him.
Then one day he went out and came to the door of the mosque, and wanted to ascend to it, but could not. He said: 'How can I go up to the mosque, O Lord?' or something like that. He intended to return to his house, but he feared that by returning he would bring grief to the people. So he forced himself to ascend to the mosque and led the people in the afternoon prayer on Friday, and he did not complete the prayer except with difficulty of breath. This was the last prayer he prayed.
Then he returned to his house and remained until the morning of Saturday the next day. His wife brought food near him, and he said to her: 'I can manage nothing, nothing(1).' She said to him: 'What is the matter with you?' He said to her: 'I have fallen behind.' Then he lost consciousness and remained in that state all day long.
Then his wife spoke to him and said: 'What made you lose consciousness?' or something close to this. He said to her: 'The angels ascended with me to the lowest heaven, and I heard a speaker saying to me: Leave what you are doing, for your term has drawn near.' Then he said: 'I cannot explain to you the rest of what I saw,' or something like that.
His wife said to him: 'What were you commanded to leave?' He said to her: 'I have left that prison (imprisoning that thing); I will never take anything from it again.'
Then he remained bedridden from then until he died. The duration of his illness was ten days, and every hour his illness increased, his pain intensified, his strength and movement weakened, and his tongue became heavy. Yet with all that he was of sound mind, neither groaning nor moaning.
(1) Al-Mallali mentioned in 'al-Mawahib al-Qudsiyya' (folio 13) that his hardship reached such an extent that he was unable to perform tayammum and the pillars of prayer, so he said: 'May Allah have mercy on Abu Hanifa, for he ruled that they drop' – meaning prayer.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a biographical narrative of Imam Sanusi's final days, as recounted by his student Muhammad ibn Umar al-Mallali. It begins with a description of Sanusi's increasing withdrawal from people in his later years. He found it difficult to go to the mosque for teaching and prayer, and sometimes only went out of shyness before those waiting. When he fell ill, he stopped going to the mosque, causing distress to the community. To spare them grief, he forced himself to lead the Friday afternoon prayer, his last prayer, which he could barely complete. After returning home, he collapsed and lost consciousness. When revived, he told his wife that angels had taken him to the lowest heaven, where a voice commanded him to leave his current state, as his death was near. He also mentioned having left 'that prison' (likely a practice or possession) permanently. The narrative notes his illness lasted ten days, during which his suffering increased but he remained conscious without complaint. A footnote adds that in his extreme weakness, he could not perform tayammum or prayer actions, and he invoked Abu Hanifa's ruling that such duties are dropped by those unable to perform them.

السماء الدنيا
التيمم
المحبس
أركان الصلاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that great scholars are immune to physical suffering or that their death is without trial.
Correction: This account shows that Imam Sanusi experienced severe pain and weakness, yet he remained patient and engaged with the community. His trial was a means of spiritual elevation.

The Death of the Scholar: A Model of Faithful End
Biographical account of the scholar's final days and death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the exemplary state of the scholar during his death throes and the significance of being mindful of the shahada at the moment of death.

Core Idea
A good death is marked by conscious awareness of Allah, not just utterance of the tongue.

This passage narrates the final three days of a great scholar, showing how he faced death with unwavering faith and presence of heart. His responses to those around him reveal profound spiritual awareness, teaching us what it means to die as a believer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage attentively
Read the entire Arabic passage and its translation without stopping. Let the narrative unfold as a story of faith at the hour of death.
Notice the rich detail describing the physical symptoms and the emotional reactions.
2
Observe the scholar's state during the throes of death
Notice the physical signs described and the reactions of those around him. Reflect on the severity of the death pangs.
The narrator could not bear to watch, yet the scholar remained patient.
3
Focus on the shahada prompting incident
The scholar's response 'Is there anything else?' reveals his deep orientation. Understand why this indicates a good death.
He was already mindful of the shahada in his heart, even if his tongue could not utter it.
4
Consider the rosary and the final words
When the rosary falls, he says 'Worship has gone.' This shows his constant connection to God even in illness.
His final prayer also asks for the ability to utter the shahada consciously.
5
Take the lesson for yourself
Ask yourself: What does this teach about the importance of being mindful of Allah at all times, especially at death?
The scholar's death inspires us to live with constant remembrance of Allah.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالكلية ، ثم تجده مع ذلك يكلم من كلمه ، ويسلم على من سلَّم عليه أو يشير له فلمَّا قَرُب أجلُهُ بثلاثة أيام دخلته سكرات الموت ، فرجع يتأوه بالقهر ويميل يميناً وشمالاً ، فنظرت إليه وقد احمرت وجنتاه وأرخيت عيناه وشفتاه ، واشتدَّ نَفَسُهُ ، وتقوَّى صعوده وهبوطه ، فلم أملك صبراً على البكاء بما عاينت من شدة مقاساته وعظيم صبره على ذلك ، ففارقته وظننتُ أنه لا يبقى تلك الليلة ، وكانت ليلة السبت ، فبقي في النزع تلك الليلة والأحد إلى بعد العصر ، فكان ابن أخيه حينئذ يلقنه الشهادة مرة بعد مرة ، فالتفت الشيخ له وقال بكلام ضعيف جداً : وهل ثَمَّ غيرها ؟! يعني : أنه رضي الله تعالى عنه ليس بغافل عنها بقلبه في هذا الوقت ، وإن كنت لم أنطق بها اللسان فحينئذٍ استبشروا بذلك ، وعرف الحاضرون أنه ثابت العقل ، ليس بغافل عن الله سبحانه وكانت بنته رضي الله عنها تقول له حينئذ : تمشي وتتركني ؟! فقال لها : الجنة تجمعنا عن قريب إن شاء الله تعالى وكانت في يده رضي الله تعالى عنه سبحة ، فلما اشتدَّ مرضه سقطت السبحة من يده ، فبقي كذلك ما شاء الله ، ثم التفت إلى السبحة فلم يجدها في يده ، فقال : مشت العبادة يا محمد ؛ يعني نفسه. وكان رضي الله عنه يقول عند موته : نسأله سبحانه أن يجعلنا وأحبتنا عند الموت ناطقين بكلمتي الشهادة عالمين بها • وتوفي رحمه الله تعالى ورضي عنه يوم الأحد بعد العصر ، الثامن عشر من جمادى الآخرة ، من عام خمسة وتسعين بعد ثمان مئة وأخبرتني والدتي رحمها الله تعالى عن بنت الشيخ رضي الله عنها أنها شمَّت رائحة المسك في البيت بنَفَسِ موت أبيها ، وشمَّته أيضاً في جسده ، والله تعالى أعلم . • نسأله سبحانه أن يُقدس روحه ، وأن يسكنه في أعالي الفردوس فسيحَهُ ، وأن يجعله ممن يتنعم في كل لحظة برؤية ذاته العلية العديمة النظير والمثال ، وأن ينفعنا به في الدنيا والآخرة ، وأن يجمعنا معه بفضله وكرمه في أعلى المنازل الفاخرة ، بجاه سيدنا ونبينا ومولانا محمد صلى الله عليه وعلى آله عدد خلقه ، ورضا نفسه ، وزنة عرشه ، ومداد كلماته قال العلامة أبو جعفر البلوي : ( ودفن بين ظهري يوم الاثنين بعده حذاءً قبر أخيه الصالح العلامة أبي الحسن التانلوتي قدس الله تعالى
In general, then you find him still speaking to whoever spoke to him, and greeting whoever greeted him or indicating to him. When his time was three days away, the throes of death entered him, so he began to groan in distress and lean right and left. I looked at him: his cheeks had reddened, his eyes and lips had drooped, his breathing had intensified, his rising and falling became forceful. I could not contain my patience for crying because of what I witnessed of his severe suffering and his great patience upon that. So I left him and thought that he would not survive that night. It was Saturday night. He remained in the death throes that night and Sunday until after 'Asr. His nephew was then prompting him with the shahada time after time. The scholar turned to him and said with very weak speech: 'Is there anything else?' Meaning that he, may Allah be pleased with him, was not heedless of it in his heart at that time, even if I did not utter it with the tongue. So then they rejoiced at that, and those present knew that he was of stable mind, not heedless of Allah, exalted is He. His daughter, may Allah be pleased with her, was saying to him then: 'You go and leave me?' He said to her: 'Paradise will gather us soon, if Allah wills.' He had a rosary in his hand, may Allah be pleased with him. When his illness worsened, the rosary fell from his hand. He remained like that for as long as Allah willed. Then he looked at the rosary and did not find it in his hand, so he said: 'Worship has gone, O Muhammad' – meaning himself. He, may Allah be pleased with him, said at his death: 'We ask Him, exalted is He, to make us and our loved ones at death utterers of the two testimonies, knowing them.' He died, may Allah have mercy on him and be pleased with him, on Sunday after 'Asr, the 18th of Jumada al-Akhirah, in the year 895. My mother, may Allah have mercy on her, told me from the scholar's daughter, may Allah be pleased with her, that she smelled the scent of musk in the house from the breath of her father's death, and she also smelled it on his body. And Allah knows best. We ask Him, exalted is He, to sanctify his soul, to lodge him in the highest expanses of Paradise, to make him among those who delight at every moment by the vision of His lofty Essence, without peer or likeness, to benefit us through him in this world and the next, and to gather us with him by His bounty and generosity in the highest noble stations, by the standing of our master, our Prophet, our Mawla Muhammad, may Allah bless him and his family, as many as the number of His creation, and as His pleasure, and the weight of His Throne, and the ink of His words. The scholar Abu Ja'far al-Balawi said: 'He was buried between noon on Monday after it, adjacent to the grave of his righteous brother, the scholar Abu al-Hasan al-Tanuluti, may Allah sanctify his soul.'
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a biographical narrative describing the final three days and death of the scholar. It begins by noting that despite his severe illness, he continued to interact with people. As his death approached, the throes of death became evident. The narrator describes his physical symptoms: red cheeks, drooping eyes and lips, intense breathing. The narrator's emotional reaction is mentioned: he could not bear to watch and left, thinking the scholar would not survive the night. The death throes lasted from Saturday night through Sunday until after 'Asr. During this time, his nephew repeatedly prompted him to recite the shahada. The scholar responded with a weak voice: 'Is there anything else?' meaning that he was already mindful of it in his heart, even if his tongue could not utter it. This reassured those present of his stable mind and consciousness of God. His daughter then asked him how he could leave her, and he replied that Paradise would gather them soon. Another incident involved his rosary falling from his hand. When he noticed it was missing, he said, 'Worship has gone, O Muhammad,' indicating that the act of using the rosary for dhikr was no longer possible. He also made a prayer at his death that God grant him and his loved ones the ability to utter the shahada at death consciously. The time of death is specified: Sunday after 'Asr, 18 Jumada al-Akhirah 895 AH. A posthumous miracle is mentioned: his daughter smelled musk from his breath and body. The passage concludes with a prayer for his sanctification and elevation in Paradise, and for benefit through his intercession, followed by a note from al-Balawi about his burial next to his brother's grave.

Correction: His response to the shahada prompting and his words to his daughter show that he was fully conscious and mindful of Allah, even if he could not speak clearly.

Key Takeaway
The scholar's death teaches us that true faith is being constantly aware of Allah, especially in the final moments, and that the shahada of the heart is as important as that of the tongue.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are in your final moments, will your heart be so absorbed in Allah that even if your tongue fails, you are still uttering the shahada within? What are you doing today to train your heart for that moment?
?
The scholar's rosary fell, and he said 'Worship has gone.' What happens when the outward acts of worship are stripped from you by illness or incapacity? Does your worship remain in your heart? Are you building a connection with Allah that survives beyond physical action?
?
His daughter asked him how he could leave her. He pointed to Paradise. If you were in his place, would your words to your loved ones be filled with such certainty of reunion? What does your current attachment to this world say about your belief in the hereafter?

Praise for Sanusi's Five Creeds
Al-Shafshawni and Ibn al-Qadi praise Sanusi's five creeds and their impact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi's five creedal works are considered among the most praised and influential in Islamic thought.

Core Idea
Sanusi's five creeds are unmatched and foundational in Islamic creed.

This lesson presents two historical testimonies that highlight the exceptional status of Imam Sanusi's five creedal works. The quotes from al-Shafshawni and Ibn al-Qadi, along with the author's commentary, show why these texts became foundational in the study of kalam and remained influential for centuries.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the historical context
Read the passage to understand that this is a praise from two historians. Notice that the passage begins with 'In the creedal series of Imam Sanusi' and then quotes al-Shafshawni.
2
Identify the five creeds
Find the list of five works: al-Muqaddima, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, and al-Kubra. Note the order and names.
3
Analyze the praise
Examine al-Shafshawni's claim that they are 'among the best works authored in Islam' and Ibn al-Qadi's statement that 'no later scholar has produced anything like them.' What does this tell you about their status?
4
Reflect on the impact
Read the author's commentary on how the Sanusiyyat 'clings to the memory of history' and had great impact in the West and East. Consider why they became so influential.

In the creedal series of Imam Sanusi. The distinguished historian al-Shafshawni said concerning Imam Sanusi: 'His five creeds and their commentaries are among the best works authored in Islam; they are: al-Muqaddima, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, and al-Kubra (1), and it is a golden word in evaluating the Sanusiyyat.' And the historian Ibn al-Qadi al-Miknasi sufficed in introducing Imam Sanusi by saying: 'The author of the creeds that no later scholar has produced anything like them (2).' And the series of the Sanusiyyat with its commentaries is something that clings to the memory of history, never to be forgotten. Despite the abundance of creedal authorship and its diversity in size and methodology, and its entry into the library of kalam with full confidence and dignity, the Sanusiyyat had the greatest impact in the Islamic West and then its East, as it entered with a firm footing.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage begins by quoting the historian al-Shafshawni, who states that Imam Sanusi's five creedal works and their commentaries are among the best works authored in Islam. He then lists the five works: al-Muqaddima, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, and al-Kubra. This is followed by a second quotation from Ibn al-Qadi al-Miknasi, who declares that no later scholar has produced anything like Sanusi's creeds. The author then adds his own commentary, describing the Sanusiyyat series and its commentaries as something that clings to the memory of history and cannot be forgotten. Despite the abundance and diversity of creedal works, the Sanusiyyat had the greatest impact in the Islamic West and then the East, entering with a firm footing.

Sanusi's Graded Series: Structure and Precedents
Sanusi's graded kalam series and its precedents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the progressive structure of Sanusi's kalam curriculum and its connection to earlier works by al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani.

Core Idea
Sanusi's graded series was both traditional and innovative.

Imam Sanusi organized his kalam texts in a carefully graded sequence, from the concise 'Muhtasar al-Mukhtasar' to the comprehensive 'Basit'. While this approach was not entirely new—having been used by scholars like al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani—Sanusi's series would become uniquely influential. This lesson introduces the structure and its precedents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the series structure
Read the Arabic passage describing the four levels of Sanusi's series. Notice the progression from very concise to comprehensive.
The passage lists: mu'htasar al-mukhtasar, then mukhtasar, then wasit, then basit.
2
Identify the precedents
The passage mentions that this graded order was not unprecedented. It gives examples from al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani. Note the titles of their works.
Al-Ghazali: Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id, al-Risala al-Qudsiyya, al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad. Al-Taftazani: Tahdhib al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam, al-Maqasid, Sharh al-Maqasid.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why would a graded series be important for teaching? How does this structure help students of different levels?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلسلة التعليم المنهجي لعلم الكلام ؛ بما تميزت عن غيرها من كتب الاعتقاد بالابتداء بمعتصر المختصر الذي تجده في وجيز متونها ، إلى المختصر الذي تراه في كبير المتون ووجيز الشروح ، إلى الوسيط ، ثم إلى سعة البسيط
هذا الترتيب البديع الذي انتهجه إمامنا السنوسي لم يكن بدعاً ؛ فقد سبق إليه من قبل أعلام علم الكلام ، فلحجة الإسلام الغزالي مثله ؛ في «قواعد العقائد » و « الرسالة القدسية » و « الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد » ، وللعلامة المحقق سعد الدين التفتازاني شِبْهُهُ ؛ في تهذيب المنطق والكلام » و « المقاصد » و « شرح المقاصد » ، وكلاهما من أقصى المشرق الإسلامي ، ولكن البديع في سلسلة الإمام السنوسي هو استحياؤه وكشفه الإلهامي عن تربع هذه السلسلة المباركة مناحي النظر والتدريس في علم العقائد في المستقبل القريب ، بل إنّ تأليفه هذه عانقتها أنظار العلماء وأقلامهم في حياته قبل موته ، كلُّ هذا كان مدعاة له أن يتفرّس وجوه طلبة المستقبل ، مُلِمّاً بضعف الضعيف وبلادة ذهنه ، وشرود النَّصف النصيف في عقله ، وكدورة وهم الذكي على حدته ونباهته ، واكتفاء الموفق بلمحته وإشارته .
كل هذا هو بعضُ ما أدركه الإمام السنوسي لتنفذ همَّتُهُ في إنشاء مدرسة كلامية لها منهاجها وأسلوبها ، تبدأ حدودها بصبيان الكتاب وعامة أهل الصنائع والحِرَف الذين لا شغل لهم بالعلم ، بل بالنسوة
اللاتي همهنَّ بيوتهنَّ وقضاء حوائجهنَّ ، لتصل إلى العالم المحقق المدقق الحريص على نفائس العلم وحُلله ، والباحث الغواص في لججه لاستخراج درره ؛ فمن صغری صغرى الصغرى » إلى « شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ، ومن تقليد أعمى إلى تحقيق أسمى ، فسبحان الفتاح على ما فتح ! وله الحمد تعالى على ما أعطى ومنح
ومكنة الإمام في علم الكلام صارت مضرب مثل ، حتى قال الشيخ أبو عمران موسى بن عقدة الأغضاوي إذا ذُكر علم الكلام : ( ما رأيتُ مَنْ غربلَ هذا العلم مثل هذا الرجل ) يعني : السنوسي
وقبل رحيل إمامنا السنوسي عن هذه الدنيا الفانية . . قرت عينه حينما رأى مؤلفاته - وعلى رأسها « سنوسياته » - قد أقبل عليها طلبة العلم ، بل إن بعض شيوخه نظر فيها واستخرج ما سُمِّي بكتاب حقائق السنوسي
أمَّا الناظر في كتب فهارس المخطوطات فسيرى الأثر الكبير الذي أحدثته ( السنوسيات » ؛ إذ أعملت العلماء أقلامها في شرح متونها . والتحشية على شروحها ، ولا سيما ( الصغرى » و « شرحها » ؛ فقد كانت لهذه العقيدة تحديداً عناية فائقة ندر أن ترى مثلها لكتاب في رحاب المكتبة الإسلامية
والبديع في منهج السنوسي في سنوسياته » المباركة هو التأليف
(۱) نقله العلامة الشفشاوني في ( دوحة الناشر » ( ص ۱۲۱ - ۱۲۲ ) .
The systematic educational series for the science of kalam; distinguished from other books of creed by starting with the concise summary (mu'htasar al-mukhtasar) which you find in the brief of its texts, to the summary which you see in the large texts and concise commentaries, to the intermediate, then to the extent of the comprehensive. This wonderful order that our Imam Sanusi followed was not unprecedented; he was preceded by the prominent figures of kalam, like the Proof of Islam al-Ghazali in his 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id', 'al-Risala al-Qudsiyya', and 'al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad'; and like the investigating scholar Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani in his 'Tahdhib al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam', 'al-Maqasid', and 'Sharh al-Maqasid'—both from the far Islamic East. But the wonderful thing in Imam Sanusi's series is his inspired foresight and unveiling that this blessed series would occupy spheres of study and teaching in the science of creeds in the near future; rather, his authorship of these works was embraced by the sight of scholars and their pens during his lifetime before his death. All this was a reason for him to discern the faces of future students, taking into account the weakness of the weak and the dullness of his mind, the distraction of the mediocre in his intellect, the turbidity of the intelligent one's imagination despite his sharpness and acumen, and the satisfaction of the successful with his glance and hint. All this is some of what Imam Sanusi realized so that his determination would penetrate in establishing a kalam school with its curriculum and style, its boundaries beginning from the children of the Qur'an school (kuttab) and the common people of crafts and trades who have no engagement with knowledge, rather with women—those whose concern is their houses and fulfilling their needs—to reach the investigating, scrutinizing scholar who is keen on the precious gems of knowledge and its adornments, and the researcher diving into its depths to extract its pearls. So from the 'Sughra Sughra al-Sughra' to the 'Sharh al-'Aqida al-Kubra', and from blind imitation to the highest verification. Glorified be the Opener for what He opened! And to Him belongs praise for what He gave and granted. And the Imam's standing in the science of kalam became a proverb, until Sheikh Abu 'Imran Musa ibn 'Uqda al-Aghdawi said, when kalam was mentioned: 'I have not seen anyone who sifted this science like this man'—meaning Sanusi. And before our Imam Sanusi's departure from this perishable world, his eye was delighted when he saw his works—especially his 'Sanusiyyat'—accepted by students of knowledge; rather, some of his teachers looked into them and extracted what was called the book 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'. As for the observer in the books of manuscript catalogs, he will see the great impact that the 'Sanusiyyat' made; for scholars employed their pens in commenting on its texts and glossing on its commentaries, especially the 'Sughra' and its commentary; indeed, this creed specifically received exceptional attention rarely seen for a book in the expanse of the Islamic library. And the wonderful thing in Sanusi's methodology in his blessed 'Sanusiyyat' is the arrangement. (1) Reported by the scholar al-Shafshawni in 'Dawhat al-Nashir' (pp. 121-122).
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and methodological introduction describing Imam Sanusi's innovative graded series of kalam textbooks. It begins by stating that his series is distinguished by its progressive structure: starting from a very concise summary (mu'htasar al-mukhtasar) in brief texts, moving to a summary in larger texts and concise commentaries, then to an intermediate level, and finally to a comprehensive exposition. Next, it notes that this graded approach was not entirely new—scholars like al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani had similar multi-level works—but Sanusi's innovation was his inspired foresight: he anticipated that his series would become central to future teaching, and indeed it attracted scholarly attention in his own lifetime. The passage then explains that Sanusi designed his series with all types of students in mind: from the weakest and dullest, to the mediocre, to the intelligent but easily distracted, to the naturally successful who need only a hint. This understanding drove him to create a kalam school whose curriculum spans from the most basic level (children in kuttab and common craftspeople, even women concerned with domestic duties) up to the advanced investigating scholar. The series thus ranges from 'Sughra Sughra al-Sughra' (the smallest of the small) to 'Sharh al-'Aqida al-Kubra' (the commentary on the Greater Creed), guiding the reader from blind imitation to the highest verification. The author then highlights Sanusi's reputation in kalam, quoting Sheikh Abu 'Imran al-Aghdawi who said he never saw anyone 'sift' this science like Sanusi. The passage concludes by noting the immediate success of the Sanusiyyat: students flocked to them, some of his teachers extracted a book called 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi', and manuscript catalogs show an enormous impact through commentaries and glosses, especially on the 'Sughra' and its commentary, which received extraordinary attention. The final sentence, though cut off, restates that the wonderful aspect of Sanusi's methodology in the Sanusiyyat is the arrangement of levels. A footnote cites the source of the quote.

معتصر المختصر

Correction: The passage explicitly states that al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani also authored multi-level works on kalam, showing that Sanusi's approach was part of a broader tradition.

?

Do you feel a responsibility to preserve and pass on this tradition, or do you learn only for yourself? How will you ensure that your knowledge benefits others?

Sanusi's Inclusive Vision and Lasting Impact
Sanusi's inclusive design and the impact of his series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi designed his kalam curriculum to include students of all levels, from beginners to advanced scholars, and the lasting impact of his Sanusiyyat.

Core Idea
Sanusi's curriculum embraced all learners, from children to scholars.

Imam Sanusi's series was not just a set of books; it was a comprehensive educational vision. He designed it to cater to every type of student, from the weakest to the brightest, and even included women and common craftspeople. His foresight paid off as his works were eagerly studied and commented upon, especially the 'Sughra' and its commentary. This lesson explores that vision and its impact.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Sanusi's inclusiveness
Read the Arabic passage that describes how Sanusi tailored his series for students of all abilities and backgrounds. Notice the diverse groups mentioned.
It mentions: weak students, mediocre, intelligent, successful, as well as children, common people, women, and advanced scholars.
2
Learn about the range and goal
The passage states the series ranges from 'Sughra Sughra al-Sughra' to 'Sharh al-'Aqida al-Kubra', and from 'blind imitation' to 'highest verification'. Reflect on this progression.
This shows the curriculum covers the entire spectrum of learning.
3
Understand the impact
The passage mentions the immediate success and enduring influence of the Sanusiyyat, especially the 'Sughra' and its commentary. Consider the scholarly reception.
Scholars wrote commentaries and glosses, and his teachers even extracted a book from his works.

هذا الترتيب البديع الذي انتهجه إمامنا السنوسي لم يكن بدعاً ؛ فقد سبق إليه من قبل أعلام علم الكلام ، فلحجة الإسلام الغزالي مثله ؛ في «قواعد العقائد » و « الرسالة القدسية » و « الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد » ، وللعلامة المحقق سعد الدين التفتازاني شِبْهُهُ ؛ في تهذيب المنطق والكلام » و « المقاصد » و « شرح المقاصد » ، وكلاهما من أقصى المشرق الإسلامي ، ولكن البديع في سلسلة الإمام السنوسي هو استحياؤه وكشفه الإلهامي عن تربع هذه السلسلة المباركة مناحي النظر والتدريس في علم العقائد في المستقبل القريب ، بل إنّ تأليفه هذه عانقتها أنظار العلماء وأقلامهم في حياته قبل موته ، كلُّ هذا كان مدعاة له أن يتفرّس وجوه طلبة المستقبل ، مُلِمّاً بضعف الضعيف وبلادة ذهنه ، وشرود النَّصف النصيف في عقله ، وكدورة وهم الذكي على حدته ونباهته ، واكتفاء الموفق بلمحته وإشارته .
كل هذا هو بعضُ ما أدركه الإمام السنوسي لتنفذ همَّتُهُ في إنشاء مدرسة كلامية لها منهاجها وأسلوبها ، تبدأ حدودها بصبيان الكتاب وعامة أهل الصنائع والحِرَف الذين لا شغل لهم بالعلم ، بل بالنسوة
اللاتي همهنَّ بيوتهنَّ وقضاء حوائجهنَّ ، لتصل إلى العالم المحقق المدقق الحريص على نفائس العلم وحُلله ، والباحث الغواص في لججه لاستخراج درره ؛ فمن صغری صغرى الصغرى » إلى « شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ، ومن تقليد أعمى إلى تحقيق أسمى ، فسبحان الفتاح على ما فتح ! وله الحمد تعالى على ما أعطى ومنح
ومكنة الإمام في علم الكلام صارت مضرب مثل ، حتى قال الشيخ أبو عمران موسى بن عقدة الأغضاوي إذا ذُكر علم الكلام : ( ما رأيتُ مَنْ غربلَ هذا العلم مثل هذا الرجل ) يعني : السنوسي
وقبل رحيل إمامنا السنوسي عن هذه الدنيا الفانية . . قرت عينه حينما رأى مؤلفاته - وعلى رأسها « سنوسياته » - قد أقبل عليها طلبة العلم ، بل إن بعض شيوخه نظر فيها واستخرج ما سُمِّي بكتاب حقائق السنوسي
أمَّا الناظر في كتب فهارس المخطوطات فسيرى الأثر الكبير الذي أحدثته ( السنوسيات » ؛ إذ أعملت العلماء أقلامها في شرح متونها . والتحشية على شروحها ، ولا سيما ( الصغرى » و « شرحها » ؛ فقد كانت لهذه العقيدة تحديداً عناية فائقة ندر أن ترى مثلها لكتاب في رحاب المكتبة الإسلامية
والبديع في منهج السنوسي في سنوسياته » المباركة هو التأليف
(۱) نقله العلامة الشفشاوني في ( دوحة الناشر » ( ص ۱۲۱ - ۱۲۲ ) .
The systematic educational series for the science of kalam; distinguished from other books of creed by starting with the concise summary (mu'htasar al-mukhtasar) which you find in the brief of its texts, to the summary which you see in the large texts and concise commentaries, to the intermediate, then to the extent of the comprehensive. This wonderful order that our Imam Sanusi followed was not unprecedented; he was preceded by the prominent figures of kalam, like the Proof of Islam al-Ghazali in his 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id', 'al-Risala al-Qudsiyya', and 'al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad'; and like the investigating scholar Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani in his 'Tahdhib al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam', 'al-Maqasid', and 'Sharh al-Maqasid'—both from the far Islamic East. But the wonderful thing in Imam Sanusi's series is his inspired foresight and unveiling that this blessed series would occupy spheres of study and teaching in the science of creeds in the near future; rather, his authorship of these works was embraced by the sight of scholars and their pens during his lifetime before his death. All this was a reason for him to discern the faces of future students, taking into account the weakness of the weak and the dullness of his mind, the distraction of the mediocre in his intellect, the turbidity of the intelligent one's imagination despite his sharpness and acumen, and the satisfaction of the successful with his glance and hint. All this is some of what Imam Sanusi realized so that his determination would penetrate in establishing a kalam school with its curriculum and style, its boundaries beginning from the children of the Qur'an school (kuttab) and the common people of crafts and trades who have no engagement with knowledge, rather with women—those whose concern is their houses and fulfilling their needs—to reach the investigating, scrutinizing scholar who is keen on the precious gems of knowledge and its adornments, and the researcher diving into its depths to extract its pearls. So from the 'Sughra Sughra al-Sughra' to the 'Sharh al-'Aqida al-Kubra', and from blind imitation to the highest verification. Glorified be the Opener for what He opened! And to Him belongs praise for what He gave and granted. And the Imam's standing in the science of kalam became a proverb, until Sheikh Abu 'Imran Musa ibn 'Uqda al-Aghdawi said, when kalam was mentioned: 'I have not seen anyone who sifted this science like this man'—meaning Sanusi. And before our Imam Sanusi's departure from this perishable world, his eye was delighted when he saw his works—especially his 'Sanusiyyat'—accepted by students of knowledge; rather, some of his teachers looked into them and extracted what was called the book 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'. As for the observer in the books of manuscript catalogs, he will see the great impact that the 'Sanusiyyat' made; for scholars employed their pens in commenting on its texts and glossing on its commentaries, especially the 'Sughra' and its commentary; indeed, this creed specifically received exceptional attention rarely seen for a book in the expanse of the Islamic library. And the wonderful thing in Sanusi's methodology in his blessed 'Sanusiyyat' is the arrangement. (1) Reported by the scholar al-Shafshawni in 'Dawhat al-Nashir' (pp. 121-122).
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and methodological introduction describing Imam Sanusi's innovative graded series of kalam textbooks. It begins by stating that his series is distinguished by its progressive structure: starting from a very concise summary (mu'htasar al-mukhtasar) in brief texts, moving to a summary in larger texts and concise commentaries, then to an intermediate level, and finally to a comprehensive exposition. Next, it notes that this graded approach was not entirely new—scholars like al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani had similar multi-level works—but Sanusi's innovation was his inspired foresight: he anticipated that his series would become central to future teaching, and indeed it attracted scholarly attention in his own lifetime. The passage then explains that Sanusi designed his series with all types of students in mind: from the weakest and dullest, to the mediocre, to the intelligent but easily distracted, to the naturally successful who need only a hint. This understanding drove him to create a kalam school whose curriculum spans from the most basic level (children in kuttab and common craftspeople, even women concerned with domestic duties) up to the advanced investigating scholar. The series thus ranges from 'Sughra Sughra al-Sughra' (the smallest of the small) to 'Sharh al-'Aqida al-Kubra' (the commentary on the Greater Creed), guiding the reader from blind imitation to the highest verification. The author then highlights Sanusi's reputation in kalam, quoting Sheikh Abu 'Imran al-Aghdawi who said he never saw anyone 'sift' this science like Sanusi. The passage concludes by noting the immediate success of the Sanusiyyat: students flocked to them, some of his teachers extracted a book called 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi', and manuscript catalogs show an enormous impact through commentaries and glosses, especially on the 'Sughra' and its commentary, which received extraordinary attention. The final sentence, though cut off, restates that the wonderful aspect of Sanusi's methodology in the Sanusiyyat is the arrangement of levels. A footnote cites the source of the quote.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi's curriculum was only for advanced scholars and not for beginners or women.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that his school includes children, common people, women, and all levels up to advanced scholars, showing inclusivity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the range of Sanusi's curriculum?
A) From 'Sughra Sughra al-Sughra' to 'Sharh al-'Aqida al-Kubra'
B) From 'Muhtasar al-Mukhtasar' to 'al-Basit'
C) From 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' to 'al-Iqtisad'
D) From 'Tahdhib al-Mantiq' to 'Sharh al-Maqasid'

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's curriculum was designed to be inclusive of all learners and had a profound impact, especially through the 'Sughra' and its commentary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi did not dismiss the weak, dull, distracted, or even women—he designed his curriculum with them in mind. Who are the people you have dismissed as incapable of deep knowledge? Will you repent from that elitism and make a concrete effort to teach someone you have overlooked?
?
Sanusi saw his works accepted by scholars and students before his death, and his eyes were delighted. What legacy are you building right now that will bring you joy in this life and the next? If you died today, what would you leave behind that truly benefits others?
?
The passage contrasts blind imitation (taqlid) with highest verification (tahqiq). Are you still content with blind imitation in your beliefs? What is one belief you hold that you have never critically examined, and what will you do this week to move toward verification?

Life of Imam al-Sanusi
intermediate

Sanusi's Unique Methodology in the Sanusiyyat
Describes Sanusi's unique methodology and impact

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key features of Imam al-Sanusi's methodology in his Sanusiyyat, particularly his layered composition (al-ta'lif al-mutarakib) and audience-tailored approach.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi's works are a layered system where each text adds new value.

Al-Sanusi's Sanusiyyat are not just a set of creeds; they represent a carefully crafted educational system. This passage describes his unique 'layered composition' methodology, where each work, while covering the same core beliefs, offers novelties in style, proofs, and examples. It also highlights how he tailored his discourse to different audiences and incorporated spiritual elements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic and English translation to get a sense of the descriptive nature and identify the main themes.
The passage begins with a description of the ideal reader and ends with a list of features.
2
Identify the key features of the methodology
Notice three main aspects: layered composition (al-ta'lif al-mutarakib), diversity of audience, and the three special features (Sufi references, supplications, poems/proverbs).
Layered composition means each work adds new content despite sharing general features.
3
Understand the impact of the Sanusiyyat
See how scholars engaged with his works: writing commentaries, glosses, and quoting from multiple commentaries selectively.
The Small Creed and its commentary received extraordinary attention.
4
Examine the misconception about repetition
The passage clarifies that repetition is not redundancy but a deliberate pedagogical strategy where each work offers something new.
This misconception is addressed in the common misunderstanding.

Those who are concerned with their homes and fulfilling their needs, so that it reaches the meticulous, verifying scholar who is eager for the precious gems of knowledge and its adornments, and the researcher who dives into its depths to extract its pearls; from the 'Smallest of the Smallest' to the 'Commentary on the Great Creed', and from blind imitation to the highest verification. So glory be to the Opener for what He has opened! And praise be to Him, the Exalted, for what He has given and bestowed. The status of the Imam in the science of kalam became proverbial, such that Sheikh Abu Imran Musa ibn Uqda al-Aghdawi said when the science of kalam was mentioned: 'I have not seen anyone who sifted this science like this man' – meaning al-Sanusi. Before our Imam al-Sanusi departed from this transitory world, his eyes were comforted when he saw his works – foremost among them his 'Sanusiyyat' – being eagerly received by students of knowledge. Indeed, some of his teachers examined them and extracted what was called the book 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'. As for one who looks in the manuscript catalogues, he will see the great impact made by the 'Sanusiyyat'; for scholars employed their pens in commenting on their texts and writing glosses upon their commentaries, especially the 'Small Creed' and its commentary; for this creed specifically received extraordinary attention rarely seen for a book in the Islamic library. The remarkable aspect of al-Sanusi's methodology in his blessed 'Sanusiyyat' is the layered composition that necessarily requires something new and beneficial; for the texts, despite their varying sizes, show repetition in their general features, but with novelty in the style of presentation, variety in proof and example, and an increase unique to each 'Sanusiyya' not found in others. How often do you see scholars quoting and writing investigations about our Imam al-Sanusi, concluding them with their saying: 'As in the Commentary on the Great Creed', or 'Commentary on the Middle Creed', or 'Commentary on the Small Creed', and they cannot suffice with just the 'Commentary on the Great Creed' instead of the others. Every observer knows the diversity of discourse in which the diversity of the audience was taken into account; for al-Sanusi had a good intention in rescuing blind followers from all segments; so you see him addressing the layman and the specialist, the young and the old, the illiterate woman and the child, in a language careful to ensure understanding and clarification, without preoccupying himself with embellishing expression. Yet despite all this, his 'Sanusiyyat' came out 'sweet in words, elegant in phrases.' Then among the remarkable features of this successful series is the purposeful and deliberate blending of some gnostic references in the manner of the Sufi masters, and some gentle supplications with which he concludes his chapters for the sake of familiarity and reminding, sharpening resolve and seeking divine success, and some poems, proverbs, and sayings that moisten the dryness of the text and soften its harshness. How great an effect this feature has, and what tranquility it spreads in souls! So how excellent is this successful editor-scholar! Imam al-Sanusi hinted at the fulfillment of this method in presenting belief as intended, and its blessing and safety from the trouble of delving into what does not concern; for
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and methodological description of Imam al-Sanusi's works and approach. It begins by describing the ideal reader of his works: a meticulous scholar and a deep researcher. It then highlights the range of his writings from the simplest 'Smallest of the Smallest' to the advanced 'Commentary on the Great Creed', indicating a progression from blind imitation (taqlid) to highest verification (tahqiq). The author praises God for this achievement. Next, it states that al-Sanusi's status in kalam became a byword, citing a quote from Sheikh Abu Imran that he had not seen anyone 'sift' the science like al-Sanusi. Before his death, al-Sanusi saw his works eagerly received by students, and even some of his teachers examined them, extracting a work called 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'. The passage then emphasizes the great impact of the Sanusiyyat in manuscript catalogues, with scholars writing commentaries and glosses, especially on the Small Creed and its commentary, which received extraordinary attention. The unique feature of al-Sanusi's methodology is described as 'layered composition' (al-ta'lif al-mutarakib) where each work, while sharing general features, introduces new elements in style, proof, and example. Scholars often quote from his various commentaries, and they cannot rely on just one commentary because each has distinct content. This shows the diversity of discourse tailored to different audiences: laymen, specialists, young, old, illiterate women, and children. His language aims at clarity and understanding without rhetorical embellishment, yet the works are sweet and elegant. Finally, the passage mentions three remarkable features of the series: (1) deliberate blending of Sufi gnostic references, (2) inclusion of gentle supplications at chapter ends for spiritual benefit, and (3) use of poems, proverbs, and sayings to soften the text. These features bring tranquility and show the effectiveness of his method in presenting creed.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The repetition in al-Sanusi's works means they are redundant and lack originality.
Correction: The passage explains that the repetition is intentional and each work contains new elements in style, proof, and example, making them distinct and necessary for different audiences.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'al-ta'lif al-mutarakib' (layered composition) in al-Sanusi's methodology?
A) Writing the same text multiple times with no changes
B) Building new works on previous ones while adding original material
C) Compiling works from different authors without attribution
D) Abridging larger works into smaller summaries

Key Takeaway
Al-Sanusi's Sanusiyyat are not duplicates but a layered pedagogical tool, each text contributing uniquely to the education of different audiences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What passes through your mind when you realize that al-Sanusi wrote different layers for different audiences, from the illiterate woman to the verifying scholar? In which of these layers do you truly live - or do you skip straight to the highest without mastering the basics? Pause and name one area of your deen where you are still in 'blind imitation' and commit to seeking the 'highest verification' starting today.
?
Al-Sanusi blended Sufi references, supplications, poems and proverbs into his creedal texts to 'moisten the dryness' and bring tranquility. Look at your own relationship with learning aqeedah: is it dry and harsh, or do you find sweetness and ease? If it is dry, what gentle reminder can you weave into your study to soften your heart and draw you closer to the One who opened his path?
?
Before he died, al-Sanusi's eyes were comforted seeing his works eagerly received. But you and I - will our efforts bring comfort to our eyes on our deathbed? Are you merely consuming knowledge or are you producing something that will outlive you? Write down one thing you will change today to ensure your legacy is not just stored books but transformed hearts.

Opposition to Sanusi's Works and His Dream of Umar ibn al-Khattab
Relates the opposition Sanusi faced and the dream of Umar ibn al-Khattab that silenced his critics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opposition al-Sanusi faced from jealous scholars and how a dream of Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab provided him with spiritual strength to overcome them.

Core Idea
A dream of Umar ibn al-Khattab silenced Sanusi's critics and affirmed divine support for his creedal works.

This lesson recounts a remarkable episode from the life of Imam al-Sanusi. When his creedal works gained widespread acceptance, some contemporaries were seized by jealousy and spoke against him. But a dream of Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab transformed his situation, silencing his detractors and vindicating his efforts. This story illustrates the spiritual resilience of scholars and the divine signs that accompany sincere service to truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall narrative.
The passage is from the historian al-Hadiki.
2
Identify the problem
Notice the reaction of Sanusi's contemporaries: jealousy and harsh criticism. Understand how this caused him distress.
3
Reflect on the dream
Consider the dream of Umar ibn al-Khattab. What was its message? How did it change Sanusi's state?
The dream is presented as a genuine spiritual event.
4
Observe the outcome
See how the critics were silenced and later acknowledged Sanusi's merit. What does this teach about divine support?
5
Connect to broader lesson
Think about the role of spiritual experiences in the life of scholars and the protection of Islamic theology.
Consider the concept of 'mercy to the Imam and to them'.

Commentary
This passage recounts the opposition al-Sanusi faced from some jealous contemporaries when his creedal works gained popularity and academic acceptance. The historian al-Hadiki narrates that many scholars criticized Sanusi harshly, causing him great grief. In a dream, Caliph 'Umar ibn al-Khattab appeared, rebuking Sanusi for fearing people instead of Allah. This dream removed Sanusi's sorrow and emboldened him to confront the critics, who subsequently fell silent and later recognized his merit. The passage ends with a truncated sentence suggesting Sanusi's ambition that drove him to the platform of something (likely teaching or writing). This anecdote serves to vindicate Sanusi's work and highlight the divine support he received.

The Unique Method of al-Jazairi's Poem
Quotation praising al-Jazairi's poem for combining rational proofs with Sufi discourses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jazairi's poem uniquely combines rational proofs and spiritual exhortations to guide both the elite and common people.

Core Idea
Al-Jazairi's poem blends demonstrative proofs with Sufi discourses for universal guidance.

This passage from al-Minhaj al-Sadid praises a poem by Imam Abi al-Abbas al-Jazairi. The author highlights how the poem joins rational arguments with spiritual calls, following the Qur'anic method. As you read, notice the appreciation for both intellectual and spiritual dimensions in guiding seekers.

The poem is said to guide both the elite (khawass) and common people (awamm).

How blessed is that way! And how sincere a guidance! For it includes universal guidance and the attainment of the desire for every successful one who seeks to reach the truth.'

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from the author of al-Minhaj al-Sadid, praising the poem of al-Jazairi. It begins by stating that the poem contains both ways of guidance: for the elite (khawass) and the common people (awamm). The poet has combined the sweetness of poetry with the presentation of rational demonstrative proofs for Islamic beliefs. Additionally, he has included Sufi discourses meant to awaken the soul and bring the weak believer into the same rank as the strong. The author then compares this method to the Qur'anic way of combining proofs and exhortation. He ends by exclaiming the blessedness of this approach.

Jealousy and the Dream of Umar ibn al-Khattab
Story of envy toward Sanusi's creed and a dream of Umar that silenced critics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dream of Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) strengthened Imam al-Sanusi in the face of opposition and led to the acceptance of his creedal works.

Core Idea
A prophetic dream can affirm divine support against criticism.

Imam al-Sanusi faced intense criticism after writing his creed. In this lesson, we read a historical account from al-Hadiki’s Tabaqat about how a dream of Umar ibn al-Khattab turned the tide, giving al-Sanusi courage and silencing his detractors. This story illustrates the role of divine affirmation in the spread of scholarly works.

1
Read the story of opposition and dream

نعم عصره ؛ قد اشتعلت نار الغيرة والحسد في قلوب بعض علماء حينما رأوا عقائد السنوسي يُكتب لها التوفيق والمسيرة التعليمية ، ولم تلبث أن خبت ؛ رحمةً بالإمام وبهم من قبل ، قال العلامة المؤرخ الحضيكي في ( طبقاته ) : ( ولما ألف بعض عقائده أنكره كثير من علماء وقته ، وتكلموا بما لا يليق ، فكثر تغيره بذلك ، ثم رأى في منامه عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه واقفاً على رأسه يتهدده على الخوف من الناس ، فأصبح وقد زال حزنه ، وقوي على المنكرين ، فخرسوا من حينئذ ، ثم أقر بفضله )
Yes, in his time, the fire of jealousy and envy ignited in the hearts of some scholars when they saw the creeds of al-Sanusi being granted success and educational progress; and it did not take long to subside, out of mercy for the Imam and for them beforehand. He woke up with his sadness gone, and he became strong against the deniers, and they were silenced from then on, and then they acknowledged his virtue.'

Commentary
This passage recounts opposition faced by Imam al-Sanusi after writing his creeds. Some scholars were jealous and criticized him. His distress was relieved by a dream of Umar ibn al-Khattab, who reproached him for fearing people. This gave him courage; his critics fell silent and eventually acknowledged his virtue.

Sanusi's Motivation and the Unique Virtue of Tawhid
Sanusi's motivation and his saying on the unique virtue of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Sanusi held the science of tawhid in such high esteem and how his dedication led to his renowned works, especially al-Sughra.

Core Idea
For Sanusi, tawhid is the supreme science because it alone yields true knowledge and fear of Allah.

Imam al-Sanusi's journey to becoming a leading authority in kalam was driven by a deep conviction: that the science of tawhid is uniquely capable of instilling knowledge of Allah and God-consciousness. In this passage, we hear his own words on the matter and see how his commitment earned him lasting recognition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic once to get a feel for its flow. Then read the English translation. Notice how the passage moves from motivation to quote to outcome.
The quote from Sanusi is the heart of the lesson.
2
Understand Sanusi's claim about tawhid
Sanusi states that among all outward sciences, only tawhid bestows knowledge of Allah and watchfulness over Him. Reflect on why he might single out tawhid.
He also says tawhid opens understanding of all other sciences.
3
Connect his motivation to his success
The passage says his good opinion and pure intention led to success. See how this connects to the praise of his works, especially al-Sughra.
His sincerity is a key theme.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question based on Sanusi's saying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولعلَّ الرغبة الجامحة التي حملت إمامنا السنوسي إلى منصَّةِ الإمامة في علم الكلام والتوحيد .. هي حسن ظنه بهذا العلم ، وصفاء نيّته في تعلمه وتعليمه ؛ تعرف هذا من قوله رحمه الله تعالى : ( ليس علم من علوم الظاهر يورث معرفته تعالى ومراقبته غير التوحيد ، وبه يفتح فهم كل العلوم ، وبقدر معرفته يزيد خوفه منه تعالى ) فحالفه التوفيق والتأييد ، فرقيَ دَرَجَ هذا العلم حتى رَأَسَ فيه وصارت تأليفه في علم التوحيد يُشار إليها بالبنان ، ووسمت بالفضل ، حتى قيل فيها : ( وعقائده كافية فيه ، خصوصاً الصغرى » ، لا يعادلها شيء من العقائد كما أشار إليه )
Perhaps the overwhelming desire that carried our Imam al-Sanusi to the platform of imamate in the science of kalam and tawhid... was his good opinion of this science, and the sincerity of his intention in learning it and teaching it. You know this from his saying, may Allah have mercy on him: 'There is no science of the outward sciences that bestows knowledge of the Exalted and watchfulness over Him except tawhid, and by it the understanding of all sciences is opened, and to the extent of one's knowledge of it, his fear of Him the Exalted increases.' So success and support befell him, and he rose in the ranks of this science until he became a leader in it, and his works on the science of tawhid were pointed to with fingers, and were marked with merit, until it was said about them: 'His creeds are sufficient in it, especially the Minor (al-Sughra); nothing else equals them among creeds,' as has been indicated.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage explains Imam al-Sanusi's motivation for his deep involvement in kalam: it was his high opinion of the science of tawhid and his pure intention. It then quotes his statement that tawhid is the only external science that yields knowledge of Allah and produces God-consciousness. Because of this, he achieved great success and leadership, and his works, especially the Minor Creed, are considered unsurpassed.

Key Terms
علم الكلام
التوحيد
العقيدة الصغرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the science of tawhid is just one of many outward sciences, equal to others like fiqh or usul in producing knowledge of Allah.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that no other outward science bestows knowledge and fear of Allah except tawhid. It is unique in this regard and is the key to understanding all other sciences.

Checkpoint

A) Worldly wealth
B) Knowledge of Allah and watchfulness over Him
C) Miracles
D) Political power

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Sanusi believed tawhid is the supreme outward science because it alone grants direct knowledge and fear of Allah, and his sincere devotion to it made his works, especially al-Sughra, revered classics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Sanusi said that the more knowledge of tawhid, the greater the fear of Allah. Reflect on your own learning: Has your fear of Allah increased with your study of tawhid? If not, what is blocking that increase, and what will you do about it before tomorrow?
?
Sanusi's sincerity of intention was the fuel for his success. Examine your intention: Why do you study tawhid? Is it for recognition, to argue, or to truly know and fear Allah? Write down one concrete change in your study habits to purify your intention starting now.

A Dream of Prophetic Endorsement: Sanusi's Creed in Paradise
A dream where Prophet Ibrahim teaches children the Minor Creed of Sanusi in Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dream narrative served as a miraculous endorsement of Sanusi's Minor Creed, reflecting the reverence for his work.

Core Idea
Even Paradise confirms the authority of Sanusi's creed.

This lesson presents a remarkable dream reported by a student of Sanusi. In it, a deceased righteous man sees Prophet Ibrahim himself teaching Sanusi's creed to children in Paradise. This story was preserved as a sign of divine confirmation of the creed's soundness and a testament to its spiritual impact.

Guided Path

Read the story

Commentary

Then a righteous man after death was seen teaching Sanusi's creed to children in Paradise alongside Prophet Ibrahim. This story serves as a miraculous endorsement of Sanusi's creed.

Why Sanusi Wrote the Sughra al-Sughra for His Father
Editor explains why Sanusi wrote the Sughra al-Sughra for his father.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific historical reason why Imam Sanusi composed the Sughra al-Sughra, namely for his elderly father who needed a shorter creed.

Core Idea
The Sughra al-Sughra was written to meet a personal pedagogical need, not for general publication.

Before we study the theological content of the Sughra al-Sughra, it is valuable to appreciate the circumstances of its composition. This passage from the editor provides a touching story from Imam Sanusi's life: he wrote this shortest creed for his own father, who struggled with the Sughra due to old age. Understanding this context deepens our appreciation of Sanusi's dedication to making Islamic creed accessible to all.

Guided Path
1
Read the background passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice how the editor builds up to the story by first praising the Sughra.
The passage is a narrative, so follow the sequence of events.
2
Identify the problem
Find the problem that led to the composition: the father could not study the Sughra easily due to old age and many preoccupations.
Look for the phrase about 'heavy upon him'.
3
Understand the solution
See how Sanusi responded by writing a shorter creed and even copying it by hand for his father.
This shows his personal care and pedagogical flexibility.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider what this story teaches us about Sanusi's approach to teaching aqeedah: he adapted the medium to the learner's capacity.
The Sughra al-Sughra is a testament to that principle.
